**The Egyptian Book of the Dead**

**Introduction to the Chapters of Coming Forth by Day**

This ancient text, known as the Egyptian Book of the Dead, contains chapters that guide the deceased through the afterlife. These chapters are recited on the day of burial to ensure the deceased's resurrection and glory. Here are some key chapters:

1. **Chapter for Coming Forth by Day and Living After Death**
2. **Chapter for Traveling on the Road Above the Earth**
3. **Chapter to Avoid Being Assigned Work in the Netherworld**
4. **Chapter to Make Funerary Statuettes Work for the Deceased in the Netherworld**
5. **Chapter for Passing Through the Void of Apepi**
6. **Chapter for Opening the Tuat by Day**
7. **Chapter for Coming Forth Victoriously**
8. **Chapter for Confronting Adversaries in the Netherworld**
9. **Chapter for Entering and Exiting the Netherworld**
10. **Chapter for Removing the God's Displeasure Against the Deceased**
11. **Hymns to Ra at His Rising and Setting**
12. **Chapter for Opening the Mouth in the Netherworld**
13. **Chapter for Remembering One's Name in the Netherworld**
14. **Chapter for Keeping the Heart in the Netherworld**
15. **Chapter for Repelling Crocodiles and Serpents in the Netherworld**
16. **Chapter for Living by the Breath of Air in the Netherworld**
17. **Chapter for Avoiding Slaughter in the Netherworld**
18. **Chapter for Not Dying a Second Time**
19. **Chapter for Escaping Corruption in the Netherworld**
20. **Chapter for Breathing Air and Commanding Water in the Netherworld**
21. **Chapter for Assuming Various Forms, Such as the Golden Hawk or the Lotus**
22. **Chapter for Uniting the Soul with the Dead Body**
23. **Chapter for Restoring Memory**
24. **Chapter for Securing the Soul from Imprisonment in the Netherworld**
25. **Chapter for Opening the Tomb to the Soul and Shade**
26. **Chapter for Sailing a Ship in the Netherworld**
27. **Chapter for Entering the Bark of Ra**
28. **Chapter for Knowing the Powers of the West and East**
29. **Chapter for Arriving at the Garden of Aaru**

These chapters are designed to help the deceased navigate the afterlife, ensuring they can live, work, and enjoy pleasures similar to those on earth.**The Egyptian Book of the Dead**

**Introduction**

When Sir Peter Le Page Renouf began publishing his translation of the Book of the Dead in 1892, he intended to include an elaborate introduction. This introduction would provide information about the book's form, history, and religious value. However, Renouf's work remained unfinished, and we are left without notes or clues about the intended introduction. To understand his views, we must refer to his Hibbert lectures from 1879.

The Book of the Dead is not a single, unified book. It is a collection of texts that evolved over time, with parts dating back to the Old Empire. The texts of the Middle Empire show various editions, and some chapters are attributed to a king of the 1st dynasty. Over centuries, the original text was modified and expanded, with new chapters added and revisions made. The book's various parts remained independent, similar to the Hebrew Psalms. The acceptance of one chapter did not imply the acceptance of the next, and it seems that the relatives of the deceased chose chapters from the collection.The Book of the Dead, before the 26th dynasty, was a collection of texts chosen by the deceased's relatives based on their preferences and budget. During the Saite period, a comprehensive revision was made, establishing a more consistent order and adding new chapters (162-165). This version, though not strictly enforced, became widely accepted. Glosses were introduced, and the number of textual variants decreased compared to earlier periods.

In 1842, Lepsius published a significant papyrus from the Turin Museum, calling it the "Book of the Dead," a term that replaced Champollion's "Ritual," which was inaccurate. The Book of the Dead is not a ritualistic text; it contains prayers and hymns spoken by the deceased, not by priests. The Egyptian title, "pert m hru," is challenging to translate, with Renouf suggesting "coming forth by day," though "coming out of the day" might be more accurate.

Renouf highlighted the difficulties in translating the text due to its simplicity, brevity, and corruption over time. Errors arose from careless scribes and the oral transmission of chapters. Some chapters were as obscure to ancient Egyptians as they are to us. Despite progress in understanding the text, many parts remain unclear, and Renouf's translation, while a significant step forward, still contains passages that may seem nonsensical to modern readers.

The Book of the Dead is deeply intertwined with Egyptian mythology and abstract concepts, often expressed through metaphors. For example, the phrase "he ate his heart" likely means "he regretted sorely." Understanding these metaphors is crucial for accurate translation, but many remain elusive. Despite these challenges, Renouf's work has made the text more accessible, though it is not definitive.Renouf hesitated to translate the Book of the Dead, a task he viewed as the pinnacle of his Egyptological work. He believed the book aimed to glorify the deceased in three ways:

1. **Renewed Earthly Existence**: The deceased enjoys a life similar to their earthly one, with the ability to use their limbs, eat, drink, and fulfill all physical needs. Sometimes, Gods assist them, enhancing their well-being and pleasure. The afterlife's bliss is mainly depicted through the joys of agricultural life.

2. **Transformation**: The deceased can traverse the universe in any form they choose, assuming any appearance they desire. These transformations are voluntary and not mandatory.

3. **Identification with Osiris and Other Gods**: The deceased is often identified with Osiris, as indicated by the prefix "Osiris" before their name. They may also be associated with other Gods, such as in Chapter 42, where each limb is linked to a different deity. This Osirian identity empowers the deceased to overcome numerous adversaries.

Additionally, the book promises the deceased complete preservation from dismemberment and decay. This concern likely stems from a time when bodies were dismembered during burial, a practice the deceased found horrifying. The frequent prayers for body reconstitution and assurances against losing any part highlight the importance of an intact body for afterlife existence. This belief underpins the practice of mummification, as decay was seen as the ultimate threat to the body's integrity.

The Book of the Dead's core teachings likely originated in Heliopolis, an ancient city with deep religious traditions. Some chapters may have come from Abydos priests, but Heliopolis is considered the primary source.

**Chapter I**: This chapter, titled "The Beginning of the Chapters of Coming Forth by Day," includes words that bring about resurrection and glory, and describe entering and exiting Amenta. It is recited on the day of burial for the victorious deceased, who speaks to the Bull of Amenta, identifying themselves with Thoth, the eternal king, and other Gods who aid Osiris in triumphing over adversaries.

The deceased claims kinship with Osiris and Horus, participating in rituals and festivals, and ensuring their soul's entry into the house of Osiris. They request the same privileges as other beneficent souls, such as receiving bread and beer, and having unobstructed access to the house of Osiris. The deceased's soul is found to be balanced and free of wrongdoing.

**Notes on Translation**: The text used for this translation is from the papyrus of Huneferu, dating back to the XIXth dynasty. The title "Chapter of coming to the divine Powers attached to Osiris" is common in older manuscripts. The divine Powers mentioned are the children of Horus, depicted standing on a lotus beneath Osiris's throne.

The words "raising" and "glorifying" are central to the text, indicating resurrection and the bestowal of glory. The deceased's body and soul are described as being raised and given life in the Netherworld, with the body being restored to its full faculties. The term "glorious" applies to both sound and light, reflecting the deceased's elevated status in the afterlife.The Book of the Dead, 5

There are, indeed, variations in the title of Chapter 17, but some of these variations are considered incorrect. The words "remember" and "confer glory" have different origins and meanings. The latter is used in religious rituals where priests identify the deceased with Osiris and other Gods. Many tomb paintings depict priests performing these rituals.

M. Deveria has shown that "ci Jiiadt-heru" means "victorious, triumphant," but the translation "veridiqiue" is incorrect. "Heru" means "voice," not "speech." In a passage from Chapter 181, "S^ I V" signifies "failure." "S^ I V\\ QA fiiailt heru" means "one whose voice is Law," a divine title, not used for living mortals. The translation "juste de voix" limits the concept to one of its secondary meanings. "Sema'at heru" is a divine act performed by Thoth through his utterances.

The particle "an" is a demonstrative, not a preposition or verb. It is used like the Latin "en" or Hebrew "hinne." For example, in the phrase "It is Thoth who is here," "an" is a demonstrative particle, not implying a verb. Egyptologists should recognize that Egyptians did not conceive verbs as we do.

The Bull of Amenta is Osiris. "Bull," like "Lion" or "Hawk," is a figurative name for Gods or kings. Osiris is sometimes depicted with a Bull's head.

The word "T'afat" is often mistranslated as "judges." Divine judges are called "t'afat," but the term is not exclusive to them. Every God and Goddess had their "t'afat," and all ancient Egyptian towns had their divine judges. In Egyptian mythology, no God stood alone; every God's actions involved others.

The "sfbmi" are enemies of the "Sjtn," either as Ra or Osiris. This name likely personifies dark clouds.

"Het Sant," or "House of the Prince," is the great Sanctuary at Heliopolis. Many geographical locations in the Book of the Dead have counterparts in the Egyptian heaven.

"Rec/iii" is a locality in northern Egypt. The mourners and weepers mentioned are mainly Isis and Nephthys.

"Teshtesh" is one of Osiris's names, possibly referring to his molten image.

The God "whose heart is motionless" is Osiris.

"Re-stau" is one of the gates of the Netherworld, specified in Chapter 17, line 19.

"Sechem" is Letopolis, where Osiris's arm was deposited when his other limbs were scattered across Egypt.

The "Tank of Flame" is where the sun rises or sets, as depicted in papyrus vignettes.

"Tenait" is the feast of the seventh day of the month.

The speaker assumes the roles of various priests, performing religious ceremonies. Egyptian priests had divine titles, and their ceremonies symbolized the Gods' acts.

The text here is corrupt. The translation follows Ag. Instead of "exalt," some manuscripts have "anoint with oil." The Turin text might be translated as "I lustrate with water in Tattu and with oil in Abydos, exalting him who is in the heights." The word "beq" means "clear, bright, shining," and the olive tree's name derives from this. The term "I make bright, illustrious, glorious" is used for the Sun in Chapter 17.

This might be said by the "Arch-seer" priest at Heliopolis.

One of Osiris's titles is "Ba," which might mean "Ram," as he was worshipped in this form in the Mendesian Nome. The word "ba" signifies "power, force," and is used for both the soul and the ram. The ram is called "ba" for its headbutting force, and the heron is called "ba" for its fish-cleaving bill. The word "ba" is not onomatopoeic but signifies "digging through, cleaving, piercing, splitting." The hieroglyphic variants are expressive of this concept.

The "sem" and "ura herp hem" were priests serving Ptah. The latter, the high priest of Ptah at Memphis, often combined his office with that of the "seftt." The ceremony involved a grand procession around Ptah's sanctuary, carrying the God's coffin on a sledge. "Sokaru" means "the coffined," and Ptah Sokaru is a form of Osiris. Details of the ceremony are in M. Mariette's Abydos, with King Seti I depicted as a Sem priest.

"Suten-henen" was called Heracleopolis by the Greeks.

The word "sej>" means "turn" and has various meanings like the Latin "vices." Later versions of this chapter include more petitions. The deceased asks to appear before the Lord of the Gods, attain the region of Madt, rise as a living God, shine like the divine host in heaven, and be one of them. They request to see the ship of the holy Sahu (Orion) in the sky, not be prevented from seeing the lords of the Tuat (Netherworld), smell the sacrificial offerings, and sit with the divine host. They ask for the Cher-heb to make invocation over their coffin, hear propitiation prayers, and for the divine ship Neshemet to advance for them, without their soul and its possessor suffering repulse. An invocation to Osiris follows.### The Book of the Dead, Chapter 17

### Invocation to Osiris

"Hail to thee, Prince of Amenta, Osiris, lord of Nifura; grant that I may advance in peace towards Amenta, and that the Lords of Tasert may receive me and say to me, 'Salutation! Salutation! in Peace!' Let them make for me a seat by the Prince of the divine Powers, let the two Chenemta Goddesses [Isis and Nephthys] receive me, in presence of Unneferu, the Victorious. Let me be a follower of Horus in Re-stau, and of Osiris in Tattu. Let me assume all forms for the satisfaction of my heart in every place that my Genius (Ka) wishes."

The following rubric is found as early as the XIXth dynasty in connection with this chapter, but it seems to have originally been attached to Chapter 72:

"If this discourse is learned upon earth, or is written upon the coffin, the deceased may come forth upon every day that he pleases and again enter his house without impediment. And there shall be given to him bread and beer and flesh meat upon the table of Ra: he shall receive allotment in the Fields of Aarru [the Elysian fields of Egyptian mythology], and there shall be given to him there wheat and barley, for he shall be flourishing as when he was upon earth."

Chapter I is followed in M. Naville's edition by another, which the learned editor calls I B. This chapter is found in so very few copies that the text cannot as yet be restored. The two texts published by M. Naville differ widely from each other. It was known, however, down to the Roman period, though not inserted into copies of the Book of the Dead. It is called Chapter of Introducing the Mummy into the Tuat on the day of burial. The 124th chapter bears a similar title. The word here translated mummy is probably not to be understood of the visible mummy, but of the living personality which it enclosed.

The chapter opens with an invocation:

"Hail to thee, who art in the sacred region of Amenta, the Osiris, [the deceased] knows thee and thy name, defend him from those Worms which are in Restau, who live upon the flesh of men and swallow their blood."

The names of the Worms were given, but in consequence of the gaps in the text, they cannot now be recovered. The chapter finishes with prayers in which the deceased identifies himself with Horus, who has taken possession of the throne which his father has given him; he has taken possession of heaven, and inherited the earth, and neither heaven nor earth shall be taken from him, for he is Ra, the eldest of the Gods. His mother suckles him and offers him her breast, which is on the horizon at Dawn.

### Vignette to Chapter IX

### Chapter II: Coming Forth by Day and Living After Death

"Oh thou Only One, who shinest from the Moon, let me come forth amid that train of thine, at large, and let me be revealed as one of those in glory. And when the Tuat is opened to the Gods, let N come forth to do his pleasure upon earth amid the Living."

Notes: This chapter occurs in only two of the ancient manuscripts collated by Naville: Ae and Pf. It is also found in the papyrus of Ani.

1. "Unicus," the Sole and Only One, is one of the many appellatives of the Sun. He is here represented as shining from the Moon.
2. "Multitude, throng, train," here put for the "heavenly host," the "akronos astron xlos" (Euripid., Fr 596), or the Hebrew "DiDlTn."
3. "Forth, out of doors, at large," in opposition to enclosure in the tomb.
4. "Disclose, unfold, reveal, make clear."
5. Or "among the Glorious ones."

### Chapter III: Another Chapter Like It

"Oh Tmu, who proceedest from Ur-henhenu, who art resplendent as the Lion-faced, and who strewest thy words to those who are before thee; Here cometh the faithful N, from the band of those who do the bidding of thy words. O ye seamen of Ra at the gloaming of the day, let N live after death, like Ra daily. Here the helmsman: As Ra is born from Yesterday, so he too is born from Yesterday, and as every God exulteth in life, so shall N exult even as they exult in life. I am Thoth as he goeth forth from the House of the Prince in Heliopolis."

Notes: The only ancient copy of this chapter is in the papyrus of Amen-neb (Ae), and here it is imperfect.

1. A personification of the Nile. The later texts read "the great Goddess in the Water."
2. The later texts have "implying the two lions Shu and Tefnut." But the older texts have a single God, with a lion's face or form. The two notions, however, are found in combination in the Pyramid texts of Unas (1. 558) and Teta (1. 332).
3. See note 8 on Chapter I.

### Chapter IV: Another Chapter, for Traveling on the Road Which is Above the Earth

"It is I who travel on the Stream which divideth the divine Pair, I am come, let there be given to me the lands of Osiris."

Notes: This fourth chapter has not as yet been found in any of the papyri of the best period.

1. Literally "weeping," "flood of tears," hence "overflow, inundation, stream or canal." It is one of the names of the Nile on earth and in heaven and of his personification as a God. See Chapter 61, and F.S.B.A., XIII, p. 8 and 9.
2. The two divinities in opposition or contrast, like Set and Horus (Chapter 17, 25) or the sister Goddesses named Merta (37, i). Thoth is the umpire between Set and Horus (Darkness and Light) and mediates between them, but he and Ra (the Moon and Sun) are spoken of as the two Rehu Gods traveling over the sky.

### Chapter V: Chapter Whereby Work May Not Be Imposed Upon a Person in the Netherworld

"Here is N. He saith, I am he who raiseth the hand which is motionless, and I come forth at the hour. I am the living Soul, and there go before me the longings of those who bring salutation."

Notes: This chapter is found in several of the best manuscripts, but the text is extremely corrupt, and must have become absolutely unintelligible. The Turin text differs greatly from that of the older copies, and the transposition of words clearly shows how little the transcribers understood what they were writing. I follow chiefly the text of Aa, the papyrus of Nebseni.

1. These words only occur in the later copies.
2. The older reading, but seems to be the more correct.
3. The oldest text must have had simply the ideographic "Ba," but Fd has "Hnemu." The "living Soul" is that of the Sun, whether he is called Ra or Osiris.
4. "Desires, wishes, loves," literally, "hearts."
5. "Salute," as in Chapter 12, i, and 14, i. The "saluter," is the name of the Ape who is seen in the vignettes of the papyri saluting the rising of the sun. See M. Naville's Todienbuch, I, plates 21 and 22; the Papyrus of Ani, plate 2; the Todienbuch of Lepsius, Chapters 16 and 126.

### Chapter VI: Chapter Whereby the Funereal Statuettes May Be Made to Do Work for a Person in the Netherworld

"O Statuette there! Should I be called and appointed to do any of the labors that are done in the Netherworld by a person according to his abilities, lo! all obstacles have been beaten down for thee; be thou counted for me at every moment, for planting the fields, for watering the soil, for conveying the sands of east and west. Here am I, whithersoever thou callest me."### Chapter VII: Passing Through the Void of Apepi

"Oh, One of Wax, who captures and seizes with force, and lives off those who are still! Let me not become motionless before you, let me not be paralyzed before you, let your venom not enter my limbs, for my limbs are the limbs of Tmu. If you would not be paralyzed, let me not be paralyzed. Let your languor not enter these limbs of mine. I am the One who presides over the pole of Heaven, and the powers of all the Gods are my powers. I am he whose names are hidden, and whose abodes are mysterious for all eternity. I proceed from Tmu, and I am safe and sound."

**Notes:**
- Apepi represents the storm-cloud and is the enemy of Ra, who defeats him. Unlike Sutu, the Darkness of Night, Apepi is not deified.
- This chapter is recited during the journey of the heavenly boat through cloud ridges, depicted as the coils of a great serpent.
- The chapter was said over a wax figure of the demon.

### Chapter VIII: Opening the Tuat by Day

"The Hour reveals what the head of Thoth keeps hidden, who gives might to the Eye of Horus. I call upon the Eye of Horus, which gleams as an ornament on the brow of Ra, the father of the Gods. I am that Osiris, the Lord of Amenta, and Osiris knows his day, and that it is his lot to end his being and be no more. I am Sutu, the father of the Gods, the imperishable one. Stay, Horus, for he is counted among the Gods."

**Notes:**
- Thoth is a personification of the moon, and the relations of solar and lunar phenomena are sources of much Egyptian mythology.
- The passage is difficult but implies the transition from Light to Darkness and vice versa.

### Chapter IX: Opening the Tuat

"O Soul most mighty, here am I: I have come to see you. I open the Tuat to see my father Osiris and drive away the darkness. I am he whom he loves. I have come to see my father Osiris, to pierce the heart of Sutu, and to perform all duties to my father Osiris. I open all the paths in heaven and on earth. I am the son who loves his father, and I come as a mummified one, glorious and well-equipped. O all ye Gods and Goddesses, the path is made for me."

**Note:**
- "Soul most mighty" is a principal name of Osiris. The chapter is spoken in the person of Horus, the son of Osiris.

### Chapter X: Coming Forth Victoriously

"I come forth victoriously against the adversaries. I cleave the heaven, I open the horizon, and I travel over the earth on foot. The Glorious and the Great ones come forward to me, for I am furnished with countless Words of Might. I eat with my mouth, and I chew with my jaw; for I worship the God who is Lord of the Tuat, and that is given to me which endures amid overthrow."

### Chapter XI: Coming Out Against the Adversary in the Netherworld

"Here is the Osiris N. O Eater of his arm: away from his path! I am Ra coming forth from the horizon against his adversary, who shall not be delivered from me. I have stretched out my hand, as the Lord of the Crown, and lifted my feet. I shall not be given up; my adversary shall fall before me; he has been given up to me and shall not be delivered from me. I rise up like Horus, I sit down as Ptah, I am victorious as Thoth, and powerful as Tmu: I walk upon my feet, I speak with my mouth, searching for him who has been given up to me; he shall not be delivered from me."

**Note:**
- The Eater of his arm represents Darkness, which is destroyed by the Sun.

### Chapter XII: Entering and Coming Forth Out of the Netherworld

"Salutation to you, O Ra, who guards the secrets of the gates over this domain of Seb, and this Balance with which Ra raises up Maat daily: Here am I, who cleaves open the earth, grant that I may come and advance in age."

**Notes:**
- This chapter occurs only in Pa among the older manuscripts and is repeated as Chapter 120 in the Turin copy.
- Maat is treated as a proper name in many places.
- The sun is represented as an infant at dawn and as an aged man at sunset.

### Chapter XIII: Entering After Coming Out from Amenta

"I enter as a Hawk and come forth as a Bennu at Dawn. Let the way be made for me that I may adore Ra at the fair Amenta, and the locks of Osiris. I urge on the hounds of Horus. Let the way be made for me that I may adore Osiris, the Lord of Life."

**Notes:**
- The Bennu is a bird of the Heron kind, often mistakenly identified with the Phoenix.
- The passage is corrupt in both ancient and recent forms.

### Chapter XIV: Removing Displeasure from the Heart of the God Against the Deceased Person

"Hail to you, oh God who sends forth the Moment, who presides over all the Secret things, and protects the utterance of my words. Here is a God displeased against me; let wrong be overwhelmed and fall upon the hands of the Lord of Law. Remove the impediments in me and the evil and the darkness, oh Lord of Law, and let that God be reconciled to me, removing that which detains me from you. Oh, lord of offerings in Kenu, let me offer to you the propitiatory offering by which you live, and let me live by it and be reconciled. Let all the displeasure in your heart against me be removed."

**Notes:**
- There is a significant difference between the earlier and later texts of this chapter.
- The older texts are more intelligible, while the Turin text is largely unintelligible.
- The chapter's title has been misunderstood by former translators.### Chapter XV: Hymn I

Adoration to Ra, who rises from the eastern horizon of Heaven. Those who accompany him praise him. Here is the Osiris N, the Victorious, and he says:

"O radiant Orb, who rises each day from the Horizon, shine upon the face of Osiris N, who adores you at dawn and honors you at dusk. Let the soul of N come forth with you into heaven, journey in the Maatit boat, and finish his course in the Sektit boat until he reaches the stars in heaven."

He says, invoking his Lord, the Eternal One:

"Hail to you, Horus of the Two Horizons, who is Chepera, Self-originating. Beautiful is your rising from the horizon, enlightening the two Earths with your rays. All the Gods rejoice when they see you, the King of Heaven, with the Nebt Unnut established upon your head and the diadems of the South and North upon your brow. Thoth abides at the prow of your bark to destroy all your adversaries. Those who dwell in the Tuat come forth to meet your Majesty and gaze upon your beautiful semblance. I too come to you to be with you and see your Orb each day; let me not be detained or repulsed. Let my limbs be renewed by contemplating your glories, like all your servants, for I am one who honored you on earth. Let me reach the Land of Ages and gain the Land of Eternity, for you, my Lord, have destined them for me."

The Osiris N says:

"Hail to you who rise from the Horizon as Ra in union with Maat; you traverse heaven in peace, and all men see you as you go forward. After being concealed from them, you present yourself at the dawn of each day. Brisk is the bark under your Majesty. Your rays are upon men's faces; the golden glories cannot be told, and your beams are indescribable. The Lands of the Gods and the colors of Punit are seen in them, so men may estimate what is hidden from their faces. Alone are you when your form rises upon the Sky; let me advance as you advance, like your Majesty, without pause, O Ra, whom none can outstrip. A mighty march is yours; leagues by millions and hundreds of thousands are traveled in a small moment, and you go to rest. You complete the hours of the Night as you have measured them out. When you have completed them according to your rule, day dawns. You present yourself at your place as Ra, rising from the Horizon."

The Osiris N says, as he adores you when you shine:

"He says to you when you rise at dawn, exalting your appearance: You come forth, most glorious one, fashioning and forming your limbs, giving birth to them without labor, as Ra rising in heaven. Grant that I may attain the Heaven of eternity and the abode of your servants; let me be united with the venerable and mighty Chu of the Netherworld; let me come forth with them to see your glories as you shine at dusk, when your mother Nut enfolds you. When you turn your face to the West, my hands are in adoration to your setting as one who lives; for it is you who have created Eternity. I have set you in my heart unceasingly, who are mightier than all the Gods."

The Osiris N says:

"Adoration to you, who rise out of the Golden and give light to the earth on the day of your birth. Your mother brings you forth upon her hands, that you may give light to the whole circumference which the Solar Orb enlightens. Mighty Enlightener, who rises in the Sky and raises the tribes of men by your Stream, giving holiday to all districts, towns, and temples, and raising food, nourishment, and dainties. Most Mighty one, master of masters, who defends every abode of yours against wrong. Most Glorious one in your Evening Bark, Most Illustrious in your Morning Bark. Glorify the Osiris N in the Netherworld, grant that he may come into Amenta without defect and free from wrong, and set him among the faithful and venerable ones."

"Let him be united with the souls in the Netherworld, let him sail about in the country of Aarru after a joyful journey. Here is the Osiris N. Come forth into Heaven, sail across the firmament, and enter into brotherhood with the Stars. Let salutation be made to you in the Bark, let invocation be made to you in the Morning Bark. Contemplate Ra within his Ark and propitiate his Orb daily. See the Ant fish in its birth from the emerald stream, and see the Abtu fish and its rotations. And let the offender fall prostrate when he meditates destruction for me, by blows upon his backbone. Ra springs forth with a fair wind; the Evening Bark speeds on and reaches the Haven; the crew of Ra are in exultation when they look upon him; the Mistress of Life is delighted at the overthrow of the adversary of her Lord. See Horus at the Look-out of the ship, and at his sides Thoth and Maat. All the Gods are in exultation when they behold Ra coming in peace to give new life to the hearts of the Chu, and here is the Osiris N along with them."

[Litany]

"Adored be Ra as he sets in the Land of Life. Hail to you, who have come as Tmu, and have been the creator of the cycle of the Gods. Hail to you, who have come as the Soul of Souls, August one in Amenta. Hail to you, who are above the Gods and who lightens up the Tuat with your glories. Hail to you, who come in splendor and go round in your Orb. Hail to you, who are mightier than the Gods, who are crowned in Heaven and King in the Tuat. Hail to you, who open the Tuat and dispose of all its doors. Hail to you, supreme among the Gods, and Weigher of Words in the Netherworld. Hail to you, who are in your Nest, and stir the Tuat with your glory. Hail to you, the Great, the Mighty, whose enemies are laid prostrate at their blocks. Hail to you, who slaughter the Sebau and annihilate Apepi."

[Each invocation of this Litany is followed by]

"Give delicious breezes of the north wind to the Osiris N. Horus opens; the Great, the Mighty, who divides the earths, the great one who rests in the Mountain of the West, and lightens up the Tuat with his glories and the Souls in their hidden abode, by shining into their sepulchers. By hurling harm against the foe, you have utterly destroyed all the adversaries of the Osiris N."

### Hymn II

The Osiris N says when he adores Ra, the Horus of the Two Horizons, when setting in the Land of Life:

"Adoration to you, O Ra: Adoration to you, O Tmu, at your coming in your beauty, in your manifestation, in your mastery. You sail over the Heaven, you travel over earth, and in splendor, you reach the zenith; the two divisions of Heaven are in obeisance to you and yield adoration to you. All the Gods of Amenta are in exultation at your glory. Those whose abodes are hidden adore you, and the Great Ones make offerings to you, who for you have created the soil of earth. They who are on the Horizon convey you, and they who are in the Evening Bark transport you, and they say: 'Adoration at the approach of your Majesty, Come, Come, approach in peace. Oh to you, Welcome, Lord of Heaven, King of Akerta.' Your mother Isis embraces you, seeing in you her son, as the Lord of Terror, the All-Powerful, as he sets in the Land of Life at night. Your father Tatunen carries you, and his arms are stretched out behind you, and that which has taken place is made fast upon earth."

"Wake up from your rest, your abode is in Manu. Let me be entrusted to the fidelity which is yielded to Osiris. Come, O Ra, Tmu, be adored. Do your will daily. Grant success in the presence of the cycle of the mighty Gods. Beautiful are you, O Ra, in your Horizon of the West; O Lord of Law, in the midst of the Horizon. Very terrible are you, rich in attributes, and great is your love to those who dwell in the Tuat."

To be said when Ra sets in the Land of Life; with hands bent downward.

### Hymn III

Adoration to Tmu as he sets in the Land of Life. The Osiris N says:

"Adoration to Tmu as he sets in the Land of Life. The Osiris N says, adoring Tmu, when setting in the Land of Life and shedding his rays on the Tuat: Hail to you setting in the Land of Life, O Father of the Gods, you are united to your mother in Manu. Her two hands receive you daily. Your Majesty has part in the house of Sokaru. Exult because the doors of the Horizon are opened at your setting in the Mountain of the West. Your rays run over the earth to enlighten the dwellers in Amenta. Those who are in the Tuat worship you with loud acclaim and cherish hope when they see you daily. You grant the Gods to sit upon the earth; to those who follow you and come in your train. O august Soul, who begets the Gods and invests them with your attributes; the Unknowable, the Ancient One, the Mighty in your mystery. Be your fair face propitious to the Osiris N, oh Chepera, Father of the Gods. Freedom forever from perdition is derived through this Book, and upon it, I take my firm stand."

"He who has written it has spoken it, and his heart rests on the reward. Let there be given me armfuls of bread and drink, and let me be accompanied."The fifteenth chapter, as it appears in the later version (represented by the Turin Todtenbuch), is quite recent. It is essentially a collection of texts that were originally separate: (1) a hymn to Ra at sunrise, (2) a litany, (3) a hymn to Ra at sunset, and (4) a hymn to Tmu at sunset, followed by a statement about the spiritual significance of the document. There are no ancient copies of the last hymn, but the other three compositions are found, more or less complete, as far back as the 19th dynasty. The differences between the ancient texts show that the scribes had a lot of freedom in their compositions, suggesting they didn't have sacred and canonical documents in front of them. M. Naville found it necessary to publish four different versions of the hymn to the rising sun and three of the hymn to the setting sun. The ideas and expressions in these hymns are common in the religious texts of the 18th and 19th dynasties. In this translation, I followed the form adopted by the later version, correcting the text when necessary using copies from better periods.

1. The text of the Papyrus of Ani has been used as the basis for the translation of Hymn I. It is the only ancient text that gives the hymn in the form later acknowledged as canonical.

2. From the earliest period, the sun was depicted as traveling across the sky in a boat, called the "Mandjet" in the morning and the "Mesektet" in the evening. The stars that never set but are always above the horizon were called "akhemu-seku."

3. The word "akhemu" has often been taken as a mere negative, but it appears by itself with the sign of stars as a determinative. The term is also written with different symbols. One of the meanings of "akhemu" is "to turn," and it is not improbable that the stars received this name because of their small size compared to the Sun and Moon. They were what Horace called the "lesser lights." The Sun and Moon are referred to in Genesis 1:16 as the "Two Great Lights."

4. Both the Eastern and Western horizons are mentioned in this chapter, but "Horus of the Two Horizons" does not refer to this distinction. Whatever the Sun passes through or over is always conceived as double. The "Two Earths" simply imply the Earth as divided by the passage of the Sun above it. M. Grebaut discovered this important key to many Egyptian expressions.

5. "Kheper," like the German "Werden," primarily means "turning," hence "becoming." It never means "creating." "Kheper t'esef" is equivalent to the Greek "autophyes," and like that word, it is sometimes used for spontaneous productions of the mineral kingdom, such as salt or natron, as opposed to artificial products of the same nature. It cannot be used for plants, as they have an origin in something external to themselves.

6. The "Land of the Gods" and "Punt" refer to the countries lying east of Egypt. When it is said that Gods "come from Punt," it does not mean they are of Arabian origin, but simply that the Sun, Moon, Stars, and Daylight rise in the East. "Ex oriente Lux."

7. In many places, the divine name "Nut" has the determinative sign of the sky. This might be an oversight by the scribe or further proof that the Egyptians believed in a sky below the horizon.

8. The Ant and the Abtu are sometimes depicted beside the solar bark. From the egg of the Abtu rises the great Cat, the Sun. As "abtu," meaning "month," is phonetically similar to "abtu," the Tortoise, and the latter is characterized by its rotation, the word "abtu," whether applied to "month" or "tortoise," clearly signifies "the revolving one." Our modern words "Tortoise," "Tortue," and "Tortuga" express the turning or twisting of the creature's feet. In some texts, the word is written differently.

9. The Look-out of the ship, in Egyptian "nefrit," is written differently in the Papyrus of Ani. This variant is valuable as it explains a word whose existence has been questioned and tells us the Egyptian name for the seat of Horus at the prow of the Solar Bark.

10. The Litany translated here is from the Turin Todtenbuch. It is found, though in a very damaged condition, in the Papyrus of Nechtuamon at Berlin, a manuscript of the 19th dynasty. Another Litany, preceding Hymn I, is found in the Papyrus of Ani. It is addressed to "Osiris, the everlasting Lord, Unneferu, Horus of the Two Horizons, of many forms and mighty attributes. Ptah Sakru, Tmu in Heliopolis, Lord of the Unseen World, who built up Memphis and its Gods."

11. "The Land of Life," one of the names given to the realm of Osiris after death, is not mentioned in the earlier manuscripts of the Book of the Dead. Instead of "resting in the Land of Life," the older texts have "in Amenta" or "in life in Amenta," or "one living in Amenta."

12. The word "pai" implies going round like a wheel or in a circle, hence "going round on high with the Sun." It is used synonymously with "never" and "the first time, the beginning of time." A sacrificial cake is called "pai" because of its shape, similar to the Latin "rotundula." Like the Greek "kuklos," the word comes to signify a circle of persons. This circle is not necessarily of Gods. The Bremner Papyrus in the British Museum mentions an "apage" not only to Apepi, who was no God, and to his soul, body, ghost, shadow, and children, but also to his "entourage." The term "pai" should express the "feast of the New Moon," though there are difficulties on the subject. It also expresses the number nine. The Egyptian conception of the number nine might be because it is a round number, three times three. The same expression meaning "circle of Gods" and "nine Gods" led to the belief that the circle consisted of nine Gods, which was then expanded to groups of eighteen or twenty-seven. The best theory is given at the beginning of Chapter 17.

13. The Turin text seems better suited as the basis for the translation.14. This passage is challenging, but the readings are consistent. Brugsch translates it as "the Talisman of the Earth," and Pierret as "the salvation of the earth." Both interpretations are valid, but we need to consider the full context. The phrase literally means "the back of the earth." In Latin, we say sinus, gremiuin, and viscera terrae. The Egyptians also refer to the back of Seb, from which plants grow. In a text quoted by Duemichen, the Earth is substituted for Seb. Therefore, "Soil of the Earth" seems to be the best translation.

15. "Thy mother Isis." The Turin text has Nut, which doesn't fit with what follows. Other texts give different names like Tatunen, Tunen, or Tanen, which are associated with the God also known as Ptah, Sokru, and Osiris. Horus, the son of Osiris and Isis, seems to be addressed here.

16. This rubric is not found in older manuscripts. I regret suggesting 'Ennead' as a translation, which has caused confusion. The term itself is correct, but it has led to misunderstandings. 

17. This hymn hasn't been found in older manuscripts. A corrected text from the Louvre papyri is available in M. Lefebure's work.

18. "Chepera, father of the Gods." Such expressions can be misleading. They don't imply that 'father of the Gods' is a unique attribute of Chepera. Many Gods are referred to as 'father of the Gods' in different contexts. In Egyptian mythology, any God can be called this, similar to how any point in space can be the origin in mathematics.

19. When Lepsius divided the Book of the Dead into 165 chapters, Chapter 16 was just the Vignette of Chapter 15. The Vignettes from the Papyrus La of Leyden, showing the Rising and Setting Sun, are included in this translation. The Rising Sun is saluted by six Cynocephalous Apes and two kneeling Goddesses, identified as Isis and Nephthys. The Setting Sun is saluted by three hawk-headed and three jackal-headed divinities, the Spirits of Pu and Nechen. Below, the Sun of Yesterday and the Sun of Today are saluted by Isis and Nephthys.

20. Chapter XVII: "I am he who closeth and he who openeth, and I am but One. I am Ra at his first appearance. I am the great God, self-produced; His Names together compose the cycle of the Gods; Resistless is he among the Gods. I, who am Osiris, am Yesterday and the kinsman of the Morrow. A scene of strife arose among the Gods when I gave the command. Amenta is the scene of strife among the Gods. I know the name of the great God who is here. Herald of Kft is his name. I am the great Heron who is in Heliopolis, who presides over the account of whatsoever is and of that which cometh into being."

21. "Who is that? It is Osiris who presides over the account of all that is and all that cometh into being, that is Endless Time and Eternity. Endless Time is Day and Eternity is Night. I am Amsu in his manifestations; there have been given to me the Two Feathers upon my head."

22. "Who is that, and what are his Feathers? It is Horus, the avenger of his father, and the Two Feathers are the Urasi upon the forehead of his father Tmu. I have alighted upon my Land, and I come from my own Place."

23. "What is that? It is the Horizon of my father Tmu. All defects are done away, all deficiencies are removed, and all that was wrong in me is cast forth. I am purified at the two great and mighty Lakes at Sutenhunen, which purify the offerings which living men present to the great God who is there."

24. "Who is that? It is Ra himself. Which are the two great and mighty Lakes? The Lake of Natron and the Lake of Maat. I advance over the roads, which I know, and my face is on the Land of Maat."

25. "What is that? The road upon which father Tmu advances when he goes to the Field of Aarru, approaching the land of Spirits in Heaven. I come forth through the Teser gate."

26. "What is that? This gate of the Gods is Haukar. It is the gate and the two doors and openings through which father Tmu issues to the Eastern Horizon of Heaven. O ye who have gone before! Let me grasp your hands, me who become one of you."

27. "Who are they? Those who have gone before are Hu and Sau. May I be with their father Tmu, throughout the course of each day. I make full the Eye when it waxes dim on the day of battle between the two Opponents."

28. "What is that? The battle of the two Opponents is the day upon which Horus fights with Sut, when he flings his filth upon the face of Horus, and when Horus seizes upon the genitals of Sut, for it is Horus who does this with his own fingers. I lift up the hairy net from the Eye at the period of its distress."

29. "What is that? The right Eye of Ra in the period of its distress when he gives it free course, and it is Thoth who lifts up the net from it. I see Ra when he is born from Yesterday, at the dugs of the Mehurit cows."

30. "His course is my course, and conversely mine is his. What is that? Ra and his births from Yesterday at the dugs of the Mehurit cows? It is the figure of the Eye of Ra, at his daily birth. And Mehurit is the Eye. I am one of those who are in the train of Horus."

31. "What is that 'one of those in the train of Horus'? Said with reference to whom his Lord loves. Hail, ye possessors of Maat, divine Powers attached to Osiris, who deal destruction to falsehood, ye who are in the train of Hotepeschaus, grant me that I may come to you. Do away the wrong which is in me, as ye have done to the Seven Glorious ones, who follow after the Coffined one, and whose places Anubis hath fixed on that day of 'Come thou hither'!"

32. "Hotepeschaus is the divine Flame assigned to Osiris for burning the souls of his adversaries. I know the names of the Seven Glorious ones who follow the Coffined one, and whose places Anubis hath fixed on the day of 'Come thou hither.' The leader of this divine company, 'An-ar-ef, the Great' is his name; 2, Kat-kat; 3, the Burning Bull, who lives in his fire; 4, the Red-eyed one in the House of Gauze; 5, Fieryface which turns backwards; 6, Dark Face in its hour; 7, Seer in the Night."

33. "I am he whose Soul resides in a pair of Gods. It is Osiris, as he comes to Tattu, and there finds the soul of Ra; each embraces the other, and they become Two Souls. The pair of Gods are Horus, the Avenger of his Father, and Horus, the Prince of the City of Blindness. I am the great Cat, who frequents the Persea tree in Heliopolis, on that night of battle wherein is effected the defeat of the Sebau, and that day upon which the adversaries of the Inviolate God are exterminated."

34. "Who is that great Cat? It is Ra himself. For Sau said, He is the likeness (Maau) of that which he has created, and his name became that of Cat (Maau). The night of conflict is the defeat of the children of Failure at Elephantine. There was conflict in the entire universe, in heaven and upon the earth. He who frequents the Persea tree is he who regulates the children of Failure, and that which they do."

35. "O Ra, in thine Egg, who rises up in thine orb, and shines from thine Horizon, and swims over the firmament without a peer, and sails over the sky; whose mouth sends forth breezes of flame, lighting up the Two Earths with thy glories, do thou deliver me from that God whose attributes are hidden, whose eyebrows are as the arms of the Balance upon that day when outrage is brought to account, and each wrong is tied up to its separate block."The God with eyebrows like the arms of a balance is "he who lifts his arm." Deliver me from the Wardens of the Passages with harmful fingers, who serve Osiris. These Wardens are the Powers that fend off the adversaries of Bd. May your knives not harm me; may I not fall into your traps, for I know your names. My path on earth is with Ra, and my ultimate goal is with Osiris. Let your offerings not be against me, oh Gods on your altars! I am one of those who follow the Master, a keeper of the writ of Chepera. I fly like a Hawk, I cackle like the Smen-Goose, and I move eternally like Nehebkau.

Oh Tmu, who resides in the Great Dwelling, Sovereign of all Gods, deliver me from the God who feeds on the damned, whose face is like a hound but whose skin is human. This God, known as the Eternal Devourer, consumes shades and human hearts at the pool of fire. He is unseen. 

Oh Fearful one, ruler of the Two Earths, Red God who commands the execution block, to whom the Double Crown and the title of Prince of Sutenhunen are given. Osiris was chosen as the Leader among the Gods on the day the Two Earths united before the Inviolate God. The junction of the Two Earths is the head of Osiris's coffin, the beneficent Soul in Sutenhunen, the provider of food and destroyer of wrong, who has determined the paths of eternity. It is Ka himself. Deliver me from the God who seizes souls and consumes all filth and corruption, whether in darkness or light. Those who fear him are powerless.

Oh Chepera, who is in the midst of your bark and whose body is the cycle of the Gods forever, deliver me from the inquisitorial Wardens to whom the Inviolate God has given the task of guarding his adversaries and inflicting slaughter in the place of annihilation. May I not fall under your knives, may I not sit in your dungeons, may I not come to your places of extermination, may I not fall into your pits. May none of the things that the Gods abominate be done to me, for I have passed through the place of purification in the middle of the Meskat, where the Mesit and Tehenit cakes are given in Tanenit. The Meskat is the place of scourging in Sutenhunen, and the Tehenit is the Eye of Horus. Tanenit is the resting place of Osiris.

Tmu builds your dwelling, the Lion-faced God lays the foundation of your house as he goes his round. Horus offers purification, and Sut gives might, and vice versa. I have come upon this earth and taken possession with my two feet. I am Tmu, and I come from my own Place. Back, oh Lion with a dazzling mouth and head bent forward, retreat before me and my might. I am Isis, and you find me as I drop my hair loosely on my brow. I was conceived by Isis and begotten by Nephthys. Isis destroys what is wrong in me, and Nephthys removes what is rebellious. Dread follows me, and Might is in my hands. Countless hands cling to me. The dead and the living come to me. I defeat my adversaries' clients and spoil those with darkened hands. I have made an agreeable alliance. I have created the inhabitants of Cher-abat and those of Heliopolis.

Every God fears the Terrible, the Almighty one. I avenge every God against his oppressor, shooting my arrows at him when he appears. I live according to my will. I am Uat'it, the Fiery one. Woe to those who rise against me! What is this? "Of unknown attributes, which Hemen has given" is the name of the Funereal Chest. "The Witness of that which is Lifted" is the name of the Shrine. The Lion with a dazzling mouth and head bent forward is the Phallus of Osiris, or Ra. I, who drop the hair loosely on my brow, am Isis when she conceals herself, letting her hair fall over herself. Uat'it the Fiery is the Eye of Ra. Woe to those who rise against me; they are the associates of Sut as they approach.

The seventeenth chapter is one of the most remarkable in the collection, preserved from times before the XIIth dynasty. The earliest monuments have handed it down with scholia and commentaries interpolated into the text. Some monuments allow us to distinguish the original text from the additions, often written in red. However, only one text suppresses the additions, offering the most ancient form of the chapter, found on the wall of Horhotep's tomb. The sarcophagus of Horhotep contains a copy of the text with the additions. The chapter was already of venerable antiquity at that time. Other copies come from the sarcophagi of Hora, Sit-Bastit, Mentuhotep, and Sebek-aa, published by Lepsius. The British Museum has Sir Gardner Wilkinson's copy of the texts on Queen Mentuhotep's coffin and a fragment of a prince named Hornefru's coffin. These witnesses from the best period unfortunately do not agree, showing the progress of corruption long before. The variants are not just orthographic differences but different readings, mainly in the scholia. Even when explanations are identical, the form differs. The latest recensions retained the form, while ancient texts added equivalent words. Horhotep's text omits them altogether, showing they were additions to the text and scholia. The chapter grew more obscure, calling for new explanations. Manuscripts of the new empire provide fresh, ancient matter, though proof is lost due to the poor condition of literature before the XVIIIth dynasty. The XVIIIth dynasty inherited but did not invent the new form of the Book of the Dead, with its traditional vignettes. Later manuscripts show an authoritative recension, making the text more certain but not necessarily more true or intelligible. The notes and explanations reached their fullest extent. It would take a volume to translate all forms of the chapter. Here, the earliest forms of the text and first commentaries are given, showing the difference between text and later additions, all of extreme antiquity, some two thousand years before Moses. Later manuscripts will be referred to in the notes. The early title is simple, but from the XVIIIth dynasty, it resembles the first chapter's title, adding that the deceased "takes every form he pleases, plays draughts, sits in a flower, comes forth as a soul living after death, and is glorified for what is done on earth."

The remarkable opening of the chapter is an addition, unknown to the primitive recension on Horhotep's tomb walls but found elsewhere. The texts do not agree. "I am He who closes and He who opens, and I am but One." 'He who closes' is Tmu, 'He who opens' is Unen. As the God who closes and opens is one, 'I am but One' is a natural ending, appealing to classical and higher authority. "I am Alpha and Omega, the beginning and the ending, saith the Lord." The Egyptian meaning is clear, but readings differ due to the absence of determinatives in the oldest style. Horhotep and other texts have a compound word, possibly signifying the same.The text discusses various interpretations and translations of ancient Egyptian religious texts, particularly focusing on the "Book of the Dead." It highlights the differences in readings and meanings across different manuscripts and periods. Here's a modernized version of the content:

---

The term "closer and opener" appears in various forms across different texts. For instance, the papyrus of Nebseni uses a third-person form, which doesn't change the meaning but stands out as an isolated reading. Later versions, like the Turin Todtenbuch and the Cadet papyrus, emphasize that the "Opener" is a God. The absence of a determinative after certain symbols doesn't negate the sense of "opener," especially in mythological contexts where physical meanings are prioritized over metaphysical ones.

In my essay on the Myth of Osiris Unnefer, I discussed these meanings in detail. The later versions of the chapter, such as those found in the hieratic papyrus T. 16 of Leyden, change the opening to "I am Atmu, who made the Sky and created all that has come into being."

The later texts add an interpolation about the Sun's first appearance when Shu raised the Sky from Chemennu, destroying the "Children of Failure." This act is often depicted in pictures where Shu, the son of Ra, parts the Earth (Seb) and the Sky (Nut) at sunrise, earning the title "The Lifter up of the Heaven." Chemennu, known to the Greeks as Hermopolis, is referred to mystically here, meaning "Eight," possibly alluding to eight elemental deities.

The "children of Failure" are elements of darkness that vanish with the arrival of Day. This mythological expression appears later in genuine portions of older texts. The doctrine of Nomina Numina, asserting that the names of Ra, the Sun-God, form "the cycle of the Gods," is emphasized. These names, representing solar phenomena, are considered the limbs or members of Ra.

The papyrus of Nebseni shows the corruption of the passage "I am he who closes and he who opens, and I am but One," with later texts adding ambiguous words that could mean the God was alone "in heaven" or "as Heaven." The papyrus of Ani attempts to clarify with "I was born from Nu," but these improvements don't reflect well on the exegetical skills of Egyptian theologians.

The term "kinsman of the Morrow" literally means "I know the Morrow," with "Yesterday" representing Osiris and "Morrow" representing Ra. The earliest texts use either "speak" or "command," indicating that strife among the Gods began at Ra's bidding, symbolizing the natural forces coming into conflict.

The bird "bennu," identified with the Sun-God, is connected to the verb of motion "ben," meaning "going round." The Sun is thus called "bennu," similar to the Greek term "kuklophoros" in the Orphic hymns. The term "Unnefer" is often misinterpreted as "good being," but it more accurately means "that which is and that which is not yet."

The name "Amsu" is confirmed by various texts, despite some discrepancies in manuscripts. Horus, the avenger of his father, refers to his father as Tmu, not Osiris, in some texts. The two Feathers are often interpreted as the Eyes of the God, with Isis and Nephthys rising as two kites.

The term "rehit" refers to the present generation, contrasting with the past and future generations. The lakes mentioned in the texts are named "Eternity" and "the Great green one," representing the lake of Natron and the lake of Maat.

The gate depicted in the vignette shows the Sun-God passing through, with later explanations suggesting it was the gate Shu used to raise Heaven or the gate of the Tuat. Hu and Sau, sons of Tmu, are Solar appellatives, representing attributes like "the Nourisher" and "the Knowing One."

The Eye, representing the Sun or Moon, is obscured during an eclipse, with the "hairy net" symbolizing the shadow passing over the heavenly body. The Cat, associated with the Sun, is explained through a play on words, with the Egyptian name for the animal resembling the word for "shining."

Nehebu-kau, the son of Seb and Renenut, is linked to the verb "nehbu," meaning "to carry, sustain, support," and the plural of "ka," which can signify the divine or human spirit.

---

This modernized version aims to make the ancient text more accessible and understandable to contemporary readers.The God mentioned is one of the forty-two judges of the dead. In some versions of the Book of the Dead, he is described as coming from a source often associated with the Nile. The serpent symbol frequently used in his name further supports this identification with the Nile. The Pyramid texts describe him as "Water" and "of many windings."

The Devourer has similar functions to a strange animal called Amemit in the Psycho-Stasia pictures. Later texts add that the Devourer comes from the 'basin of Punit,' the Red Sea, and mention other names like 'Flint,' stationed at the gate of Amenta.

M. Maspero's interpretation of a word related to 'health' is confirmed by papyri, which state that Thoth not only freed the Eye from darkness but also restored it to life, health, and strength without damage.

Mehurit is explained as 'the Eye,' but it actually refers to the Sky, from which the Sun is born daily. The sign of plurality after Mehurit indicates the daily succession of skies from which Ra is born.

The 'coffined One' is Osiris, as stated in later texts. The 'Seven glorious ones' who follow the coffin are linked to the constellation of the Great Bear, which revolves around the Pole. The Polar Star is identified with Osiris's coffin. The names of these stars vary, and they also appear as the Seven Cows and their Bull in other texts.

The 'Red-eyed' cow is said to reside in a 'house of gauze,' possibly a cobweb. The papyri note that the "day of Come thou hither" represents the moment when Osiris invites Ra to join him, symbolizing their meeting in Amenta.

The term 'possessor of completeness' refers to Osiris when he is restored after being dismembered. He is called Ra-Tmu-Neberi'er in the great Harris papyrus.

Baba, described as the firstborn of Osiris, is a fearsome God from whom the deceased prays for deliverance. His name implies thorough searching or probing, akin to a miner's work, reflecting his role in the judgment of the dead.

The Mesqat is a place of scourging, associated with violent treatment by beating. In the 72nd chapter, the deceased prays not to perish at the Mesqat. The word also signifies 'a hide,' linking the concepts of flaying and punishment. Anubis, behind the chest containing Osiris's remains, is said to be the purifier in later texts.

The chapter transitions to the 18th chapter, where various speakers identify themselves as Tmu, Isis, and Uat'it, among others. These localities are in heaven, not on earth. Uat'it, the 'pale one,' refers to the Dawn, specifically the fiery dawn.

Hemen, a rarely mentioned divinity after the Middle Empire, is depicted with a Snake (the River) in his hand.

The last line of the chapter is damaged in all the best papyri. In the papyrus of Ani, the chapter is unfinished. Later papyri conclude by stating that the speaker is granted the power to destroy the souls of his adversaries by those in Tattu.

Chapter XVIII begins with the An-mdut addressing the Great Circles of Gods in Heaven, Earth, and the World below, presenting N as void of offense and seeking daily presence with the Gods. N prays for bread, the right to appear at the tables of the Lords of Maat, and the ability to enter and exit the Netherworld without repulse.

The Se-meri-f addresses the Circle of Gods in Restau, seeking bread, water, air, and an allotment in the Sechit-hotepu like Horus. N prays for an abiding place in the Netherworld and the receiving of cakes before the Gods.

A litany follows, invoking Thoth to make N triumphant over adversaries, as Osiris was, before various Circles of Gods in Heliopolis, Tattu, Sechem, Pu and Tepu, the Two Regions of Rechit, and Abydos. Each invocation references specific events and deities associated with these locations."Let N be victorious over his enemies, just as you make Osiris victorious over his enemies, before the Great Circle of Gods on the Highway of the Damned, on the night when judgment is passed on those who are no more. The Great Circle of Gods on the Highway of the Damned includes Thoth, Osiris, Anubis, and Astes. Judgment is passed on the Highway of the Damned when the case against the souls of the Children of Failure is closed.

Oh Thoth, who makes Osiris victorious over his enemies, let N be victorious over his enemies, just as you make Osiris victorious over his enemies, before the Great Circle of Gods at the Great Hoeing in Tattu, on the Night of Hoeing in their blood and ensuring Osiris's victory over his enemies. The Great Circle of Gods at the Great Hoeing in Tattu includes the associates of Sut, who take the forms of goats and are slain before the Gods there, with their blood running down, according to the judgment of the Gods in Tattu.

Oh Thoth, who makes Osiris victorious over his enemies, let N be victorious over his enemies, just as you make Osiris victorious over his enemies, before the Great Circle of Gods in An-arer-ef on the Night of Hiding him who is Supreme in Attributes. The Great Circle of Gods in An-arer-ef includes Shu, Babai, Ra, and Osiris, and the Night of Hiding him who is Supreme in Attributes is when they are at the Coffin, the Thigh, the Head, the Heel, and the Leg of Unneferu.

Oh Thoth, who makes Osiris victorious over his enemies, let N be victorious over his enemies, just as you make Osiris victorious over his enemies, before the Great Circle of Gods in Restau on the Night when Anubis lies with his hands upon the objects behind Osiris, when Osiris is made to triumph over his enemies. The Great Circle of Gods in Restau includes Osiris, Horus, and Isis. The heart of Horus rejoices, the heart of Osiris is glad, and the two Parts of Heaven are satisfied when Thoth ensures N's victory before these ten Great Circles around Ra and Osiris and the Circles of Gods attached to every God and Goddess before the Inviolate God. All his enemies are destroyed, and all that was wrong in him is also destroyed.

If the person recites this chapter, they will be purified and come forth by day after their death, taking any form they wish. If this chapter is recited over them, they will be prosperous on earth, safe from every fire, and no evil thing will approach them, with undeviating regularity for infinite times.

Notes:
The eighteenth chapter is one of the earliest copies of the Book of the Dead, found on the wooden coffins of the 'Old' and 'Middle' Empires, with the most complete ancient copy on the coffin of Queen Mentuhotep of the eleventh dynasty. It consists of a litany addressed to Thoth, invoked to secure the triumph of the departed against their enemies in the presence of the Gods of certain localities. Each petition references a mythological event and is supplemented by the enumeration of the Gods presiding at the named locality, sometimes with a short comment on the myth. The order of petitions varies in later versions, and the text has undergone other alterations. Copies of this chapter are extremely numerous, especially in later periods. The chapter begins with petitions to Thoth, with the preceding portion found only in the Papyrus of Ani. However, the vignette belonging to this portion appears in the great Leyden Papyrus of Kenna, indicating the text was not confined to a single manuscript. It is particularly valuable for illustrating the ritual use of portions of the Book of the Dead.

The deceased is presented to the Gods by two priests in succession, one called An-maut-ef and the other Se-meri-f. Both names are titles of Horus, and it is common for Egyptian priests to bear divine titles, with their ritual observances being dramatic and symbolic representations of the actions of the Gods. An-maut-ef literally means 'column (support) of his mother.' Horus is called 'the An-maut-ef of the Great Company of the Gods' and 'the An-maut-ef of Osiris.' Se-meri-f means 'the Beloved Son,' and the priest of this name personified Horus in his dutiful offices to his father Osiris. The translation of 'the son who loves him' instead of 'the son he loves' is incorrect, as the correct meaning is 'the son he loves.'

The word 'Circle of Gods' is a collective noun, never found in any other sense. The ancient form renders it probable that the later form is not phonetic but ideographic of roundness. This concept is found in the word for 'head' or 'top of the head,' akin to the Latin vertex, from the same root as vertere. The sign, which often appears as the determinative in later texts, originates from the cursive form of carelessly written. Instead of the word for 'wall,' we also find a sign that is ideographic of enclosure. This word occurs in the Pyramid Texts under the form, which M. Maspero translates as 'the Great Enclosure of On.' The etymological relationship to the Coptic word for 'head' has led some scholars to translate the Egyptian word as 'chiefs, princes,' but these are secondary applications of 'head.' The word implies an association of persons, hence the translation as 'Circle of Gods.'

The Eve's Provender is a corruption of the ancient term, which had probably ceased to be intelligible. According to the pantheistic system, the deceased, through identification with the Sun, absorbed and consumed all that came in their way, expressed in a somewhat brutal style. Men and Gods disappear before Unas, who makes his breakfast at dawn on great Gods, his dinner on Gods of middling quality, and his supper at even on minor deities. The word for 'things' has a wide application, frequently meaning 'property, estate,' and sometimes 'suit.'

On the last day of the month of Choiak, the great solemnity of setting up the Tat as the symbol of Osiris was observed down to the latest periods. The tablets of Pasherenptah, high priest of Ptah at Memphis, speak of this great dignitary as the king's deputy in 'Raising the Tat.' Brugsch has published a picture showing Amenophis III helping to raise the Tat, with the queen Ti and the royal princesses taking part in the ceremony. The procession is described as marching four times around the sanctuary of Ptah-Seker-Osiris.

Horus in the Dark, or Blindness, or Invisibility, is mentioned in my note.

Pu and Tepu are named together in the earliest texts as one locality, recognized by Brugsch as the metropolis of the northern nome called by the Greeks.

The feast derives its name from the words 'Come thou to me,' said of a legendary incident. The early papyri read, but this is no objection, as the sign is the determinative of the entire group giving its name to the feast.

The dead refers to those who have died 'the second death.'

The vignette from the now lost Papyrus Busca represents 'the Great Hoeing in Tattu.' The long text at Dendera contains directions for the festival commemorating the ancient myth. Two black cows are put under a yoke of wood, the plough is of tamarisk wood, and the share of black bronze. The plougher goes behind, with a cow led by a halter. A child with a lock attached to its head scatters the seed in the field of Osiris, a piece of land with given dimensions. Barley is sown at one end, spelt at the other, and flax between the two. The Cher-heb in chief recites the Office for the Sow."Chapter XIX: The Crown of Triumph

Your father, Tmu, has prepared for you this beautiful Crown of Triumph, a living diadem loved by the Gods, so you may live forever. Osiris, Prince of Amenta, ensures your victory over your enemies. Your father, Seb, has decreed you as his heir, to be celebrated as Triumphant, Horus, son of Isis and Osiris, on the throne of your father Ra, through the defeat of your adversaries. He has granted you the Two Earths, unconditionally. Atmu and the Cycle of the Gods have repeated the glorious act of Horus's triumph, son of Isis and Osiris, forever.

Osiris, Prince of Amenta, unites the Two Parts of Heaven. All Gods and Goddesses in heaven and on earth join in ensuring the Triumph of Horus, son of Isis and Osiris, over his enemies before the Great Circle of Gods in Heliopolis. Horus proclaims this four times, causing all adversaries to fall, be overthrown, and slaughtered. Their abode is transferred to the slaughtering block of the East, where they are decapitated, their necks crushed, and their thighs cut off, given to the great Annihilator in the Valley, ensuring they never escape Seb's custody.

This chapter is recited over a consecrated crown placed on the deceased's face, with incense on the flame, ensuring their triumph over all adversaries, dead or alive, making them a follower of Osiris. They will receive food and drink in the presence of this God. Recite it at dawn twice; it offers great protection with unwavering regularity for infinite times.

Notes:
The nineteenth chapter is a recent version of the eighteenth, with manuscripts dating back to the Greek period. It originates from the practice of placing garlands or floral crowns on mummies. The mummy of Aahmes I, the first king of the eighteenth dynasty, was found with a garland of pretty pink Delphinium flowers around its neck. Remains of such crowns are found in museums. For more details, refer to Dr. Pieyte's paper, "La Couronne de la Justification," in the second volume of the Transactions of the Oriental Congress held at Leyden in 1884.

Chapter XX: Another Chapter of the Crown of Triumph

This chapter is a tabulated form of chapter 18, with the rubric. The person should recite this chapter and purify themselves with natron water. They will come forth by day after death, take any form they wish, and escape from the fire, with unwavering regularity for infinite times. The earliest example of this tabulated form is found on the Berlin Sarcophagus of Mentuhotep.

Chapter XXI: The Mouth of a Person in the Netherworld

Hail to you, Lord of Light, Prince of the House encircled by Darkness and Obscurity. I come to you glorified and purified. My hands are behind you; your portion is that of those who have gone before you. Give me my mouth so I may speak with it and guide my heart in its hour of Darkness and Night.

Notes:
The oldest papyrus containing this chapter is that of Ani. The translation is based on it, but the text differs from those on the ancient coffins of Pleru and Set-Bastit, copied by M. Maspero, and from later texts. The second paragraph seems to be spoken by the God, while the first and third are from the deceased. "My hands are behind you" implies protection. On the coffins, the invocation is addressed to "one whose head is clothed with radiant white, of the House of Darkness and Obscurity." The ancient text continues, "Come to me, glorified and purified; let your hands shine with your head. Give me my mouth."

Chapter XXII: Another Chapter of the Mouth in the Netherworld

I shine forth from the Egg in the unseen world. Let my mouth be given to me so I may speak with it in the presence of the great God, Lord of the Tuat. Let not my hand be repulsed by the Divine Circle of the great God. I am Osiris, Lord of Restau, at the head of the Staircase. I come to do the will of my heart, out of the Tank of Flame, which I extinguish when I come forth.

Notes:
This chapter belongs to the earliest epoch and is one of those copied by Wilkinson from the coffin of Queen Mentuhotep. In the Papyrus of Ani, it is followed by chapter 21 as its conclusion, and both chapters are appended to chapter 1 before its rubric. The Egg in the unseen world is the Sun's globe below the horizon. The 17th chapter addresses "Ra in your Egg, who rises in your orb, and shines from your Horizon."

Chapter XXIII: The Mouth of a Person in the Netherworld

Let my mouth be opened by Ptah, and let the muzzles on my mouth be loosed by the God of my domain. Let Thoth come, equipped with Words of Power, and let him loose the muzzles of Sutu on my mouth. Let Tmu lend a hand to fling them at the assailants. Let my mouth be given to me. Let my mouth be opened by Ptah with the steel instrument he uses to open the mouths of the Gods. I am Sechit Uat'it, who sits on the right side of Heaven. I am Sahit, encircled by the Spirits of Heliopolis. All the Words of Power and accusations against me are countered by the Gods, unitedly.

Notes:
1. Osiris. The term "God of the domain" refers to Osiris at Abydos.
2. The word translated as "steel" is discussed in M. Deveria's dissertation, "Le Fer et l'Aimant." The Ceremonies of the Opening of the Mouth at the tomb are described in the Introduction to this translation.
3. The Goddess's name is phonetically written as Sechit.Chapter XXIV: Words of Power in the Afterlife

I am Khepera, self-created, on my Mother's thigh. The speed of bloodhounds is given to those in Heaven, and the spirit of hyenas to those in the Divine Circle. I gather my Words of Power from every corner, more persistently than hounds of chase and more swiftly than Light. O you who guide Ra's Bark, your rigging is sound and free from disaster as you pass to the Tank of Flame. I collect my Words of Power from every corner, for everyone it concerns, more persistently than hounds of chase and more swiftly than Light. These Words of Power create Gods from Silence or reduce them to inactivity, and impart warmth to the Gods.

Notes:
This chapter is ancient, found on the coffins of Horhotep and Queen Mentuhotep. The translation adheres closely to the oldest texts, though they are not entirely trustworthy. The term "thigh" is usually translated this way, aligning with depictions of the Goddess Nut as the Sky with the divine Scarab. The word also signifies running water, a river, or a canal. The names of the animals in the texts vary, but they imply speed or ferocity. The term "ferocity" here means high, unbridled spirit, not something negative. The Bennu bird appears in later texts, but earlier ones use a different word implying tenacity or obstinacy, virtues of a high order. The idea of Silence as the origin of Gods is also found in Greek thought and early Gnostic beliefs.

Chapter XXV: Remembering One's Name in the Afterlife

Let my name be given to me in the Great House. Let me remember my name in the House of Flame on the Night when the Years are counted and the Months are reckoned. I am He who dwells in Heaven and sits on the Eastern side of Heaven. If any God does not follow me, I will utter his name at once.

Notes:
Every Egyptian Temple symbolized Heaven, with its Great House and House of Flame as the most sacred areas. The Great House occupied a central position, like the Lady Chapel in cathedrals, and the House of Flame stood beside it.

Chapter XXVI: Receiving the Heart in the Afterlife

He says: Heart of mine, come to me in the place of Hearts! Whole Heart of mine, come to me in the place of Whole Hearts! Let me have my Heart so it may rest within me. I will feed on the food of Osiris on the eastern side of the meadow of amaranthine flowers. Give me a boat for descending the stream and another for ascending. I go down into the boat where you are. Give me my mouth to speak, my feet to walk, and my arms to overthrow my adversaries. Let two hands from the Earth open my mouth. Let Seb, the Erpa of the Gods, part my jaws, open my eyes, and give motion to my hands. Let Anubis give strength to my legs so I may stand. Let Sechit the divine one lift me up so I may rise in Heaven and issue my commands in Memphis. I possess my Heart, my Whole Heart, my arms, and my legs. I do whatever my Genius wills, and my Soul is not bound to my body at the gates of Amenta.

Notes:
The Egyptian texts have two names for the Heart, often used synonymously but sometimes distinguished. The term "Heart" is connected with lively motion, similar to the Greek and Indo-European terms for heart. The word also denotes the heart with the lungs attached. The term "whole heart" is used to describe this. The phrase "meadow of amaranthine flowers" refers to a plant mentioned in medical prescriptions and sacred texts, identified with the Coptic amaranth. The term "Erpa" is a title for Seb, the keeper of the deceased, and cannot be translated without losing its original meaning.Chapter XXVII: Keeping the Heart in the Afterlife

O Gods who seize hearts and pluck out whole hearts, and who reshape a person's heart based on their deeds, let my heart be forgiven by you. Hail to you, Lords of Everlasting Time and Eternity! Do not tear my heart from me with your fingers. Do not reshape my heart based on the evil things said against me. This heart of mine is the heart of the God of mighty names, the great God whose words are in his members, and who gives free course to his heart within him. His heart is the most insightful among the Gods. Heart of mine, I possess you, I am your master, and you are with me; do not fall away from me. I am the one you must obey in the Netherworld.

Notes:
1. There is significant variation between manuscripts from different periods. The translation of certain terms has evolved over time, with some older texts omitting particles found in later versions. The God of mighty names is Thoth, and later texts refer to him as the great God in Hermopolis.
2. The term "new, fresh, young, vigorous" is used in some readings.

Chapter XXVIII: Keeping the Heart in the Afterlife
