Oh clouded but strong one, who sails over the back of Apepi, firm-headed and steadfast when emerging from fiery blows, let me sail the ship and secure my gear. This place is empty, where the starry ones fall headlong and cannot rise. The path is narrow like the tongue of Ra. The Patrol who pilots the Double Earth, Seb abides by their rudders. The divine Form reveals the Solar Orb, and He presides over the Red ones. Let me be brought in as a distressed mariner, and let my Soul, my brother, go to that place you know. "Tell me my name," I say. The Mooring post: "Lord of the Double-Earth in the Shrine" is your name. The Blade of the Rudder: "Leg of Apis" is your name. The Hawser: "The Side-Lock which Anubis fastens on to the swathing work" is your name. The Stern or Stem Posts: "The two columns of the Netherworld" is your name. The Hold: "Akar" is your name. The Mast: "Bearer of the Great One while she passes" is your name. The Keel: "Backbone of Apuat" is your name. The Mast-head: "Throat of Emsta" is your name. The Sail: "Nut" is your name. The Leathers: "Made of the hide of Mnevis, which Sutu scorched" is your name. The Oars: "Fingers of the elder" is your name. The Bracement: "Hand of Isis, stanching the blood" is your name.

### Chapter C: The Book of Strength and Embarkation

This is the book that makes the glorified one strong and allows them to embark on the boat of Ra, along with those who are with the God. Let me guide the divine Heron to the East, Osiris to Tattu. Let me open the caverns of Hapu, clear the path of the Solar Orb, and tow Sekaru on his sledge. Let the Great One give me strength at her fixed hour. I hail and worship the Orb, joining those in adoration. I am one of them. Let me be a second to Isis, and let her glorified ones give me strength. Let me secure my gear, stop the adversary, and force him to turn back. Let Ra lend me his two hands, and let not his divine Boatmen prevent me. Let my strength be that of the divine Eye, and conversely. [As to the sundering of me in the Bark of Ra, let the sundering be as that of the Egg and the Tortoise.]

This should be said over the Figure in the Text, written on clean paper with artist's ink, fresh and mixed with essence of Anta. Let the dead person have it placed on their body without inserting it into their limbs. They will enter the Bark of Ra at the round of each day. Thoth will appreciate them, on their coming forth or entering, undeviatingly for infinite times.

### Chapter CI: Safeguards of the Bark of Ra

Oh, you who are devoid of moisture when coming forth from the stream and who rest upon the deck of your Bark: as you proceed in the direction of Yesterday and rest upon the deck of your Bark, let me join your boatmen. I am a powerful Chu. Oh Ra, in your name of Ra, since you pass through an Eye of seven cubits, whose pupil is of three: make me sound. I am a powerful Chu, let your soundness be my soundness. Oh Ra, in your name of Ra, since you pass through those who are perishing headlong: keep me standing on my feet. I am a powerful Chu, let your soundness be my soundness. Oh Ra, in your name of Ra, since you open the secrets of the Ammehit, which gladden the hearts of the Divine Circle: give me my heart. I am a powerful Chu, let your soundness be my soundness, and the soundness of your limbs be the soundness of my limbs.

This should be written with gum mixed with colors on a strip of royal papyrus and placed at the throat of the deceased on the day of burial. If these phylacteries are placed at their throat, they will rise as one of the Divine Circle and be united with the followers of Horus, while their Lamp is made firm by Isis in heaven beside Sothis. They follow Horus who resides in Sothis. Their Shade becomes divine as well as human. Vegetation grows from their body through the Goddess Menkit. They become a God forever, and their limbs are made vigorous in the Netherworld through Thoth, who did the same for Osiris, causing the light to rise from his dead body, undeviatingly and for infinite times.

### Chapter CII: Entering the Bark of Ra

Oh Great One in your Bark, let me be lifted into your Bark. Let me make head for your staircase. Let me have charge of those who convey you, who are attached to you, who are of the Stars that never set. That which I abominate, I eat not: and that which I abominate is Dirt. Let me not eat of it, but of peace offerings and Art-offerings, by which I shall not be upset. Let me not approach it with my hands, let me not tread upon it with my sandals, because my bread is of the white corn and my beer of the red corn of the Nile. It is the Sektit boat and the Aatit that have brought me to the food.### Chapter CIII: Opening the Abode of Hathor

I am a pure follower. O Ahi, let me become one of Hathor's followers.

### Chapter CIV: Sitting Among the Great Gods

Let me sit among the great Gods and pass through the place of the Sektit boat. The Bird-fly deity will convey me to see the great Gods in the Netherworld, and I will be triumphant in their presence.

### Chapter CV: Appeasing the Ka

Hail to you, my Ka, my coeval. May I come to you, be glorified, made manifest, and ensouled. Let me have strength and soundness. Let me bring you grains of incense to purify myself and your overflow. Do not impute the wrong assertions and resistance I have offered to me. For I am the green gem, fresh at the throat of Ra, given by those at the Horizon. Their freshness is my freshness, and the dainties of my Ka are like theirs. You who lift the hand at the Balance and raise Law to the nose of Ra, do not put my head away from me. For I am the Eye that sees and the Ear that hears. Am I not the Bull of the sacrificial herd? Are not the mortuary gifts upon me and the supernal powers above Nut? Grant that I may pass by you, purify myself, and cause the triumph of Osiris over his adversaries.

### Chapter CVI: Presenting a Largess at Hat-ka-Ptah

Oh God of nourishment, great one who presides over the mansions on high, give me bread and beer. Let me be made pure by the sacrificial joint and white bread. Oh ship of the Garden of Aarru, convey me to the bread of your canal, as my father, the Great one, who advances in the Divine ship.

### Chapter CVII: (Note)

This chapter consists of the first two lines of chapter 109. The vignette over it belongs to chapter 108. It has no separate existence in any of the papyri of the best periods.

### Chapter CVIII: Knowing the Powers of the West

Regarding the Hill of Bachau, upon which heaven rests, it is 300 cubits in length and 200 cubits in breadth. Sebak, the Lord of Bachau, is at the east of the Hill, and his temple is upon it. There is a serpent on the brow of that hill, 500 cubits in length, with three cubits of his forepart pierced with swords. I know the name of this serpent on his hill: "He who dwells in his own flame" is his name. At the close of day, he turns his eyes to Ra; there is a standing still in the Bark and a deep slumber within the ship. He swallows three cubits of the Great Water. Then Sutu is made to flee with a chain of steel upon him and is forced to vomit all he has swallowed. Sutu is put into his prison and says with Words of Power: "Away with you! Steel, which is fastened upon my hand. I remain in your prison, the Bark sails on, and you see the path; but your eyes close, your head is veiled, and I go on and stay your steps. I am the Manful one, who veils your head and cools the hollow of your hand: your strength is my strength. I am the Master of the Words of Power. Who is this who has been delivered to me? This Bright One, who comes on his belly, on his hind parts, and on the joints of his back. Lo! I come, and your might is in my hand. It is I who carry away your might, that I may come and seize upon the Tunnels of Ra who is united to me at sunset as he goes around heaven. But you are pierced with hooks, as was decreed against you of old. I know the powers of the West, they are Tmu, Sebak the Lord of Bachau, and Hathor, Mistress of Sunset."

### Notes

The chapters 108, 109, 112, 113, and 114 are similar in form, matter, style, and composition, each concerned with the divine Powers of some locality. One of these chapters is found on a monument of the Middle Empire, suggesting their ancient origins. The text published by Dr. Golenischef from the Sarcophagus at St. Petersburg shows signs of antiquity. The numerous forms in which this chapter has come down to us indicate its ancient origins.### Chapter CIII: Opening the Abode of Hathor

I am a devoted follower. O Ahi, let me join Hathor's followers.

### Chapter CIV: Sitting Among the Great Gods

Let me sit among the great Gods and pass through the place of the Sektit boat. The Bird-fly deity will guide me to see the great Gods in the Netherworld, and I will be victorious in their presence.

### Chapter CV: Appeasing the Ka

Hail to you, my Ka, my twin. May I come to you, be glorified, made manifest, and ensouled. Let me have strength and health. Let me bring you grains of incense to purify myself and your overflow. Do not hold my wrongdoings against me. For I am the green gem, fresh at the throat of Ra, given by those at the Horizon. Their freshness is my freshness, and the delicacies of my Ka are like theirs. You who balance the scales and uphold the Law before Ra, do not separate my head from me. For I am the Eye that sees and the Ear that hears. Am I not the Bull of the sacrificial herd? Are not the mortuary gifts upon me and the divine powers above Nut? Grant that I may pass by you, purify myself, and ensure the triumph of Osiris over his enemies.

### Chapter CVI: Presenting a Largess at Hat-ka-Ptah

Oh God of nourishment, great one who presides over the mansions on high, give me bread and beer. Let me be purified by the sacrificial joint and white bread. Oh ship of the Garden of Aarru, convey me to the bread of your canal, as my father, the Great one, who advances in the Divine ship.

### Chapter CVII: (Note)

This chapter consists of the first two lines of chapter 109. The vignette over it belongs to chapter 108. It has no separate existence in any of the papyri of the best periods.

### Chapter CVIII: Knowing the Powers of the West

There is a serpent on the brow of that hill, 500 cubits in length, with three cubits of his forepart pierced with swords. I know the name of this serpent on his hill: "He who dwells in his own flame" is his name. Then Sutu is made to flee with a chain of steel upon him and is forced to vomit all he has swallowed. Sutu is put into his prison and says with Words of Power: "Away with you! Steel, which is fastened upon my hand. I remain in your prison, the Bark sails on, and you see the path; but your eyes close, your head is veiled, and I go on and stay your steps. I am the Manful one, who veils your head and cools the hollow of your hand: your strength is my strength. I am the Master of the Words of Power. Who is this who has been delivered to me? This Bright One, who comes on his belly, on his hind parts, and on the joints of his back. Lo! I come, and your might is in my hand. ### Chapter CIX: Knowing the Powers of the East

I know that Eastern Gate of Heaven (the South of it is by the lake of Cha-ru, and the north of it by the stream of Reu), from whence Ra sails with favorable winds. I am the Teller in the divine ship: I am the tireless navigator in the Bark of Ra. I know those two Sycomores of Emerald between which Ra emerges, as he advances over what Shu has lifted up, to every gate through which he proceeds. I know the Garden of Aarru: the wall of it is of steel. The wheat of it is 7 cubits tall, the ears of it are 2 cubits, and the stalk of it is 4 cubits. The barley of it is 7 cubits, the ears are 4 cubits, and the stalk is 3 cubits. It is the glorified ones, each of whom is 9 cubits in height, who reap them, in the presence of the Powers of the East. I know the Powers of the East: Horus of the Solar Mount, the Calf in the presence of the God, and the Star of Dawn. A divine Domain has been constructed for me; I know the name of it; the name of it is the garden of Aarru.

### Notes

Another version of this chapter has been incorporated into chapter 149. The differences lie mainly in the order of the sentences. 

1. Favorable winds: "sailing breezes" correspond to phrases like "favorable winds," "trade winds," "tail wind," "stern wind." There is no authority from the older papyri for the sense of violent or tempestuous winds.

2. Every gate: "Ra at his rising is adored by the Powers of the East. They are the ones who effect the rising of Ra, by opening the door at each of the four portals of the Eastern horizon of heaven."

3. The Calf in the presence of the God: The Calf is seen in the vignettes of this chapter and also of chapter 1. The Morning Star was equally identified with Horus.

4. The divine Domain: "Nouit" designates a rural domain of considerable extent, with or without a village or house. It was a real entity, represented as a person bringing agricultural products and offerings.

### Additional Note

Later copies of the Book of the Dead add a few lines to the chapter, which were not part of the original text. The most interesting portion is as follows: "There are writings in your possession for the grant of fields of corn-land where corn sprouts from the effluxes of the God Ut'eb. The height of the corn is seven cubits, the ears are two cubits; and you shall reap it with the Glorified ones, in the presence of the Powers of the East. You shall enter boldly at the mysterious portals and be purified by those who are there." The God Ut'eb is identified with Osiris, and the text is illustrated by a picture from the temple of Philae. This concept of Osiris is of ancient origin.

### Chapter CXI

This chapter is a repetition of Chapter CVIII.

### Chapter CXII: Knowing the Powers of Pit

Oh you of corpse-like form who are in Chait and Anpit; you Goddess of the Net, who...### Chapter CXII: Knowing the Powers of Pu

Oh you of corpse-like form who are in Chait and Anpit; you Goddess of the Net, who are in Pu; ye who preside over the untilled lands, ye stars and constellations. Know ye why Pu has been given to Horus? I know it if you do not. Ra gave it to him to compensate for the blindness in his eye. Ra said to Horus, "Let me look at what is happening in your eye today," and he looked at it. Ra said to Horus, "Look at that black swine." He looked, and a grievous mishap afflicted his eye. Horus said to Ra, "My eye is as though Sutu had wounded it." And wrath consumed his heart. Ra said to the Gods, "Let him be laid upon his bed, that he may recover." It was Sutu who had taken the form of a black swine and caused the wound in Horus's eye. Ra said to the Gods, "The swine is an abomination to Horus; may he get well." And the swine became an abomination to Horus.

The circle of Gods said, who were with him when Horus came to light in his own children: "Let the sacrificial victims for him be of his oxen, goats, and swine." As for Emsta, Hapi, Tuamautef, and Kebhsenuf, Horus is their father and Isis their mother. Horus said to Ra, "Give me then two brothers in Pu and two brothers in Nechen, of this my own body; and that they may be with me as an everlasting renewal, through which the earth flourishes and storms are quenched." And his name became that of Horus upon his Column. I know the Powers of Pu: they are Horus, Emsta, and Hapi.

### Notes

1. On the situation of Pu, see chapter 18, note 6. The Pyramid Texts (Pepi I, 684) speak of the "those of the Red Crown who are in Pu."
2. Thou of corpse-like form in Chait and Anpit. Chait is the name of the 16th, or Mendesian, Nome of Northern Egypt, and Anpit was its metropolis. The God is Osiris. He is invoked in the "Lamentations of Isis and Nephthys," and asked to come to Tattu, Anpit, and Chait, which are but different names of one Sanctuary.
3. Thou Goddess of the Net. This name corresponds to the Greek Diktynna. The reason why a Goddess representing Heaven should be so called may be understood by the Homeric epithet "polynomos" applied to a net. If, however, the deity was male, according to the other reading, the reference is to "the one who was drowned in the river."
4. Ye who preside, etc. The Egyptian term is identified with the "Inlotinos" of the Demotic and Greek contracts. The remainder of this invocation is so corrupt that the sense cannot be safely guessed at.
5. See Herodotus, II, 47. The pig was not considered impure in the days of the third or fourth dynasty, when Amten, who had risen to the highest dignities, enumerates swine among the domestic animals. But long before the days of Herodotus, a change had taken place in the Egyptian religion as to the nature of Sutu. Plutarch and Aelian are to be read with caution. Some of their information is correct, but it is mixed with much error.
6. The variants are noteworthy.
7. Sacrificial victims. The substitution in Egypt of animal for human sacrifice is entirely without foundation. The supposed evidence of human sacrifices drawn from certain pictures has been misinterpreted.
8. The four children of Horus were also his brothers. He asks for two of them to be with him in each of his two cities, Pu and Nechen. The true sense of the passage is entirely lost in the later recensions and in translations made from them.

### Chapter CXIII: Knowing the Powers of Nechen

I know the Mystery of Nechen: Horus, and what his mother did for him when she herself uttered the cry: "Let Sebak, the Lord of the Marshes, be brought to us." He cast the net for them and found them, and his mother made them fast in their places. Sebak, the Lord of the Marshes, said: "I sought and found the traces of them under my fingers on the strand. I netted them in a powerful net, as the net proved to be." And Ra said: "Verily, those are fishes in the hands of Sebak, and he has found the two arms of Horus for him, which had become fishes." And Ra said: "A mystery, a mystery, in the Net." 

And the hands of Horus were brought to him and displayed before his face on the feast of the fifteenth day of the month when the fishes were produced. Then Ra said: "I grant Nechen to Horus, in the place of his two arms; that his two hands be displayed before his face in Nechen; and I grant to him whatsoever is therein comprised on the feast of the fifteenth day of the month." And Horus said: "Be it granted to me that Tuamautef and Kebhsenuf be taken with me, and that they be guards of my body in dutiful service. Let them be this under the God of Nechen." And Ra said: "Be that granted to thee, there and in Sati, and let that be done for them which is done for those who are in Nechen; yea, they are asking to be with thee." And Horus said: "Be they with thee, so that they be with me to listen to Sutu invoking the Powers of Nechen: 'Be it granted to me that I may make my entry among the Powers of Nechen.'" I know the Powers of Nechen: they are Horus, Tuamautef, and Kebhsenuf.

### Notes

1. Nechen, the chief hieroglyphic variants of which are , was situated in the third nome of Upper Egypt, and was called by the Greeks Hieraconpolis, 'city of the Hawks,' from the hawk-headed divinities mentioned in this chapter as Powers of Nechen, and of which numberless pictures are found on the monuments.
2. Between these words and those which the three old papyri Aa, Ae, and lb, which unfortunately do not agree together on all points, have a few passages here which do not appear in the later papyri. They read, "Horus and what his mother did, tossing in distressful agitation over the water." The mother then addresses persons who are not named, in words of which the sense is not clear; and Ra speaks words of which the only certain ones are "the son of Isis." Then follows the usual text.
3. This legend of Nechen is connected with that of the dismemberment of Horus, of which we have but very scanty information. It must have been like a repetition of what had happened to his father Osiris. The limbs of Horus had been thrown into the water, and when Sebak threw his net, at the prayer of Isis, he brought up two fishes, into which the arms of Horus had been turned. Reminiscences of this story are preserved in the names of several localities. "Two Fish" is the name of the Mer of the second Northern Nome, and of the pehu of the seventeenth Southern Nome; just as "Two Eyes" is the name of the pehu of the eleventh Northern Nome. The latter name may perhaps have reference to Osiris, but the same stories were probably told of both divinities.
4. On dutiful service, a word omitted in the Turin and other texts. Brugsch has discussed the sense of this word and quoted numerous passages in illustration of it. It is of course ridiculous to identify the word with the Hebrew, the meaning of which is radically different.

### Chapter CXIV: Knowing the Powers of Hermopolis

Maat is borne over the Arm, and Neith dawns at Ment'ait, and the Eye is illumined by the one who adjusts its level. I am led in by her, and I know what she brings from Kasu. I tell it not to men; I repeat it not to Gods. I come as a messenger of Ra, to make fast Maat upon the Arm, for the dawning of Neith at Ment'ait, and for restoring the Eye to him who takes the reckoning thereof. I come as omnipotent through the knowledge of the Powers of Hermopolis, who love the Powers which you love.### Chapter CXIII: Understanding the Powers of Nechen

I understand the Mystery of Nechen: Horus, and what his mother did for him when she cried out: "Let Sebak, the Lord of the Marshes, be brought to us." Sebak cast his net and found them, and his mother secured them in their places. Sebak said: "I found their traces under my fingers on the shore. I caught them in a strong net." Ra then said: "Indeed, those are fishes in Sebak's hands, and he has found the two arms of Horus, which had turned into fishes." Ra continued: "A mystery, a mystery, in the Net."

The hands of Horus were brought to him and displayed before his face on the fifteenth day of the month when the fishes were shown. Ra then said: "I grant Nechen to Horus, in place of his two arms; that his two hands be displayed before his face in Nechen; and I grant him everything within it on the fifteenth day of the month." Horus then requested: "Let Tuamautef and Kebhsenuf be with me, to guard my body in dutiful service. Let them serve under the God of Nechen." Ra agreed: "Let it be so, both here and in Sati, and let them be treated as those in Nechen; they wish to be with you." Horus then said: "Let them be with you, so they can be with me to hear Sutu invoking the Powers of Nechen: 'Let me enter among the Powers of Nechen.'" I know the Powers of Nechen: they are Horus, Tuamautef, and Kebhsenuf.

### Notes

1. Nechen, represented by various hieroglyphs, was located in the third nome of Upper Egypt and was known to the Greeks as Hieraconpolis, 'city of the Hawks,' due to the hawk-headed deities mentioned in this chapter.
2. The three old papyri Aa, Ae, and lb, which do not fully agree, have additional passages not found in later texts. They mention "Horus and what his mother did, tossing in distress over the water." The mother then speaks to unnamed individuals, and Ra mentions "the son of Isis."
3. This legend of Nechen is linked to the dismemberment of Horus, similar to what happened to his father Osiris. The limbs of Horus were thrown into the water, and Sebak, at Isis's request, caught two fishes, which were Horus's arms. This story is reflected in the names of several places, such as "Two Fish" and "Two Eyes."
4. The term "dutiful service" is omitted in some texts. Brugsch has discussed its meaning and provided numerous examples. It is incorrect to equate this term with the Hebrew word, which has a different meaning.

### Chapter CXIV: Understanding the Powers of Hermopolis

Maat is carried over the Arm, and Neith dawns at Ment'ait, and the Eye is illuminated by the one who adjusts its level. I am led in by her, and I know what she brings from Kasu. I do not tell it to men; I do not repeat it to Gods. I come as a messenger of Ra, to secure Maat upon the Arm, for the dawning of Neith at Ment'ait, and for restoring the Eye to him who takes its reckoning. I come with power through the knowledge of the Powers of Hermopolis, who love the Powers you love.

### Notes

1. The term "borne" is the same as "to gush, spring forth," but in some cases, it means "to be carried" and is written differently in Ptolemaic inscriptions. The corresponding word in chapter 116 has similar meanings.
2. The "Arm" in chapter 114 is a geographical term, like "arm of the sea." The pictures mentioned have the words "arm of the Urnes" inscribed over the stream where the Sun-God travels at night.
3. Ment'ait is the ancient reading in chapter 114, but later texts have different variations.
4. The texts differ on the reading of "illumined." The older papyri have a specific orthography, though it is somewhat suspicious.
5. Kasu, the 'Burial Place,' was the capital of the 14th Nome of Southern Egypt. Dendera is also called by different names in various texts.

### Chapter CXV: Understanding the Powers of Heliopolis

I have grown from yesterday, a Great one among the Great. I have risen above all things that come into being. The Face is revealed to the Eye of the Only One, and the darkness is broken. I am one of you. I know the Powers of Heliopolis. The All-powerful One extends a hand to us. The Gods say: "The afflicted one is heir of Heliopolis!" I know when the Lock of the Male child was made. Ra spoke with Amhauf, and a blindness came upon him. Ra said to Amhauf: "Take the spear, oh offspring of Men." Amhauf replied: "The spear is taken." Two brothers, Heb-ra and Sotem-anes, whose arm never rests, came into being. One assumed the form of a female with a lock, which became the Lock in Heliopolis. The heir of the temple is active and powerful. The flesh of his flesh is the All-seer, with divine might as the Son begotten by the Father. His will aligns with the Mighty one of Heliopolis. I know the Powers of Heliopolis: Ra, Shu, and Tefnut.

### Notes

The ancient text of this chapter is mostly lost, with only a few words remaining in fragments. The later papyri have altered the text significantly. Scholars like Mr. Goodwin and M. Lefebure have studied this chapter, but the most important work is by M. Naville. The term "All-powerful One" is a divine name, and the "Lock of the Male child" refers to the side lock borne by Horus and other priests in honor of Horus. Amhauf is a proposed emendation, and "offspring" is followed by omitting a preposition. "The flesh of his flesh" may refer to the hereditary succession of the high priest of Heliopolis.

### Chapter CXVI: Understanding the Powers of Hermopolis

Neith dawns in Mat'at, and Maat is carried upon the Arm of the Eater of the Eye by the one who reckons it out. I know it, and I am led in through the Sem priest. I do not tell it to men; I do not repeat it to Gods. I enter as one who knows not and sees not. Hail, Gods of Hermopolis. Know me as I know Neith, so the Eye may be made firm and permanent. I take delight in reckoning what is reckoned. I know the Powers of Hermopolis, who are great at the beginning of the month and diminished on the fifteenth day. They are Thoth the Unseen, Sau, and Tmu. If this chapter is known, filth is avoided, and lye is not drunk.

### Notes

This is the last chapter concerning the Powers of specific places. Despite alterations, their antiquity is undeniable. Mr. Goodwin compared them to medieval Christian legends, which are ancient but not indicative of primitive Christianity.

### Chapter CX: The Beginning of the Chapters of the Garden of Hotepit

This chapter discusses coming forth into Heaven, entering and exiting the Netherworld, and arriving at the Garden of Aarm, at the Rise in Hotepit, and the Grand Domain, blessed with breezes. It describes taking possession, being in glory, plowing, mowing, eating, drinking, and loving. Horus is seized by Sutu, who looks towards the Garden of Hotepit. Sutu releases Horus, and the double path near Heaven is opened by Sutu. Sutu takes his portion of the breeze through his own power and delivers the bowels of Horus from the Gods below. The speaker sails the great Bark on the Stream of the God Hotep, taking it at the mansion of Shu, which is renewed repeatedly. The speaker sails upon its streams to reach its domains, in unison with its changes, rules, papyrus, attendant Gods, and chieftains. The speaker reconciles the two Warrior Gods with those in charge of food and creation, and with each other. The speaker separates mourners from those who quarrel with them, stops those who are violent, and keeps the contentions of the Powers in check. The speaker seeks possession, knowledge, and mastery within the Garden of Hotep, desiring to be glorified, eat, drink, plow, reap, and love there. The speaker's mouth is potent and secured against the Glorified, and they possess a powerful Word of Power. The speaker knows the names of the domains, districts, and streams within the Garden of Hotep, and seeks to be in glory, eat, plant, reap, plow, and love there, united with the God Hotep. The speaker's mouth is armed with sharp points, and they possess the abundance belonging to the Ka and the Glorified. The speaker gives the reckoning of Shu to those who understand it, sails upon its stream, and ranges within the Garden of Hotep, for Ra is in it.### Chapter CX: The Beginning of the Chapters of the Garden of Hotepit

This chapter discusses entering Heaven, navigating the Netherworld, and arriving at the Garden of Aarm, at the Rise in Hotepit, and the Grand Domain, blessed with breezes. It describes taking possession, being in glory, plowing, mowing, eating, drinking, and loving. Horus is seized by Sutu, who looks towards the Garden of Hotepit. Sutu releases Horus, and the double path near Heaven is opened by Sutu. Sutu takes his portion of the breeze through his own power and delivers the bowels of Horus from the Gods below. The speaker sails the great Bark on the Stream of the God Hotep, taking it at the mansion of Shu, which is renewed repeatedly. The speaker sails upon its streams to reach its domains, in unison with its changes, rules, papyrus, attendant Gods, and chieftains. The speaker reconciles the two Warrior Gods with those in charge of food and creation, and with each other. The speaker separates mourners from those who quarrel with them, stops those who are violent, and keeps the contentions of the Powers in check. The speaker seeks possession, knowledge, and mastery within the Garden of Hotep, desiring to be glorified, eat, drink, plow, reap, and love there. The speaker's mouth is potent and secured against the Glorified, and they possess a powerful Word of Power. The speaker knows the names of the domains, districts, and streams within the Garden of Hotep, and seeks to be in glory, eat, plant, reap, plow, and love there, united with the God Hotep. The speaker's mouth is armed with sharp points, and they possess the abundance belonging to the Ka and the Glorified. The speaker gives the reckoning of Shu to those who understand it, sails upon its stream, and ranges within the Garden of Hotep, for Ra is in it.

### Notes

The text of this chapter handed down by the Turin papyrus and those which agree with it contains nothing very difficult for a translator, but on being compared with the older copies it is found to consist of a collection of small fragments of the older text put together without any regard to their original order or context. And about three-quarters of the old chapter are suppressed in the new recension. The editors of the fine papyrus of Sutimes in their notes upon this chapter remark that in the Turin text the sentences are in quite a different order from that of their papyrus, "On peut y voir," they say, "l'effet de lectures et de transcriptions en rebours du sens, par des scribes ayant mal compris les editions en colonnes retrogrades." This is, curiously enough, the very fault of the papyrus of Sutimes itself, which is here wrong from beginning to end, though probably derived from an excellent original. It begins with the "Isle of Corn and Barley," and jumbles together quite incoherent sentences. The oldest copy of the chapter yet discovered is that of the Tomb of Cha-em-hait, at Thebes, and by a strange fatality, it has been published in such a form that in order to read it correctly, we must begin with what is printed as line 11 and finish with line 1. We have it also in a very incomplete condition. We miss the first eighteen lines contained in the papyrus of Nebseni and the last words of every line. The papyrus of Nebseni is the only complete text we have, and here as well as elsewhere it is extremely incorrect. Some parts are so corrupt that a translation must necessarily be dependent upon conjectural emendations which can have no genuine claim upon the reader's confidence. We must be content with waiting till better authorities are discovered. The Gardens of Hotepit and Aanu are the Paradise, Elysian Fields, and Islands of the Blessed of the Egyptian imagination. They were supposed to be situated in the neighborhood of the rising Sun, but certain features were apparently suggested by the islets of the Delta.

The usual meaning of the word Hotepit, when written according to the orthography of the Pyramid Texts, is oblations, offerings. This, however, is only a derived meaning. The word really only expresses a predicate of the things offered, as putting together, uniting, reconciling; Hotep might signify Rest, or Peace; very appropriate names for such a garden. There is also a Goddess here called Hotepit, mentioned in the Pyramid inscription of Pepi I (line 423), as mother of the great Scarab: and the same name is given to Hathor in the temple of Dendera. The name of Hotep (with different determinatives) belongs to one of the islands of this blissful place. The Pyramid Texts furnish some interesting information not contained in the Book of the Dead. We are told that the approach to the Garden is over the Lake of Putrata (see chapter 40, note 1), that there is a great lake (? that of Konsit) in the middle of the Garden of Hotepit, upon which the great Gods alight, and that the Achimiu Sekiu, the starry deities who never set, there feed the departed from the wood of life upon which they themselves live, in order that he too may live. Shu and Tefnut are mentioned as divinities of this place. But perhaps the most remarkable fact is that Horus had enemies even here, who, however, were annihilated by the divine weapons at the disposal of the departed worthy, who was led there in order that "he might sit among the stars in heaven." And here it was that the beatified personage sat upon his throne of steel, which was decorated in front with faces of the lion-God, the feet of it being the hoofs of the great Bull Snta-ura, and extended his hand to the coming generation of men, whilst the Gods approached him in a submissive attitude, and made offerings to him. It was, perhaps, from these offerings that the Garden derived its name. The Garden of Aarru is often mentioned in connection with that of Hotepit, and may perhaps be considered as the most notable part of it. It is through its Gate that the Sun-God rises up into Heaven. It takes its name from a plant, a creeping, climbing, twining plant, such as the convolvulus, hop, or vine.

The term 'Garden' implies in this connection nothing more than a cultivated enclosure. The names of different localities which are invoked by the deceased and appear on the vignette of the chapter, have here been made prominent by means of heavy type.

1. Rise in Hotepit, or (later on) Hotep, is the name of one of the localities. The word has the sense of rising up, coming to light, making an appearance, and like the Greek  is especially applicable to the appearance of daybreak, or the rise of the heavenly bodies.

2. Turning. The group has the apparent sense of building, but the primitive sense is turning, as in the making of pottery.The text seems to show that "building" is not the intended meaning. This might sound confusing, but so does the original text. The papyrus of Ani forces the meaning of "day" upon a sign that would have made more sense as "turn." There were the "Portion of Sutu" and the "Portion of Horus," each representing half the world or half the day. It seems "day" is a misinterpretation, and the true meaning is that Sutu is content with his turn, freeing Horus from the underworld. The confusion of the scribes is evident in the next words. One has "As I \\N. aar," which might be "1 v\\ v\\ Nil aarni to vl di-ii." The first two groups are not phonetically identical but are related and have similar meanings. The last has been identified with the vine-branch, and in conjunction with the text, "The Vine-plant is Osiris." The Greeks identified Osiris with Dionysos. Osiris is sometimes depicted sitting under a vine with grapes hanging.

The term "he who is in Merit" or "he who is in my mouth" and "he who is in the egg" might refer to Anubis. The word "mehit" in Chapter 134 has been translated as "inkstand" or "palette," but it likely means the writing itself. Thoth is the God mentioned here. He reconciles the two Warrior Gods. The word "grind" comes from the notion of "reducing to powder," leading to "wearing away" or "decay." The term "arteries" originally meant wind-pipes, believed to convey air while veins carried blood. This error was corrected by Galen. The Egyptian doctrine of arteries conveying air is first found in the Berlin Medical papyrus.

Hesit, one of the names of Isis or Hathor, is depicted as suckling her son Horus. She is asked for "the white liquor" loved by the glorified ones, identified as milk. The name Hesit is well-attested and should not be confused with hetemit. The term "blooming" or "flowering" is used. The "longed things of Shu" are given to the deceased, who is followed by his kau. Tefait is an abode with delicacies. The phrase "he is in heaven" has been misinterpreted, but the true reading is difficult. The term "salute the stream of Teserit" is rare but correct. Teserit is a Goddess associated with Isis or Hathor. Kankanit is related to the verb of beating but not a place of punishment. Userit is a common name for Isis, especially in later texts. Smait is another appellative. The "Emerald ones" are in the emerald light of dawn. The sun rises through two sycomores of emerald. The term "purification" is related to the word for horn. The vignettes of the chapter are explained in their proper place.

Chapter CXVII discusses taking the blissful path at Restau. The speaker is the Girdled and Mighty one, coming forth triumphantly to secure his suit in Abydos and open the path at Restau. Osiris is asked to make the suit pleasant. The speaker produces the water that balances Osiris's throne and makes his way from the Great Valley. The path is made for the triumphant one. Osiris and the speaker are made triumphant over their adversaries and walk, stand, and speak before the great God, the Lord of Amenta.

Restau is one of the Gates between the Netherworld and Heaven, mentioned in later papyri. The papyrus of Ani depicts the Sun God passing through folding doors.The papyrus of Hunefer shows the God sitting between open doors, symbolizing the Gate of Passages, guarded by Osiris's attendants with "hurtful fingers" to protect against Ra's adversaries. The deceased prays for Ra's protection in Chapter 17. The ritual of being girdled is significant, as seen in various references like Unas 66 and Teta 149. The deceased prays to be girded by the Goddess Tait in Chapter 82. The phrase "coming forth triumphantly" is from the oldest texts, but later texts read "coming forth from the Crown." The "place of Maat" at Abydos refers to the final judgment of the deceased. Osiris's throne rests on water with a lotus flower and the four children of Horus. The deceased is identified with the Nile's inundation, as in Chapter 64.

Chapter CXVIII describes arriving at Restau, where the deceased is glorified by those in mummied forms at Osiris's sanctuary. Guards receive Osiris at Restau. Chapter CXIX describes entering or leaving Restau, where the deceased, as the Mighty one, worships Osiris and acknowledges his power and might. Osiris raises the deceased in power at Restau and Abydos, allowing them to traverse the heavens with Ra.

Chapter CXX is a repetition of Chapter XII, Chapter CXXI repeats Chapter XIII, and Chapter CXXII repeats Chapter LVIII. Chapter CXXIII describes entering the Great House, where Thoth balances the Divine Pair, ends their strife, and rescues the Atu from its backward course. Thoth, the measurer and regulator, establishes equilibrium between Horus and Sutu, representing Day and Night, which only occurs at the Equinoxes. The phrase "rescuing the Atu from its backward course" likely refers to the precession of the equinoxes, a phenomenon noted by ancient astronomers. The term "posterity" in this context means future generations.Chapter CXXIV: Entering the Divine Circle of Osiris

"My soul builds a Hall for me in Tattu, and I flourish in Pu. My fields are plowed by those who belong to me, making my palm tree like Amsu. I avoid abominations and filth. Peace offerings are my food, and I do not touch or tread upon filth. My bread is made from white corn, and my beer from red corn of the Nile. The Sektit or Atit boat brings them to me, and I eat under the Tamarisk's foliage. I know the beauty of the arms that announce Glory for me and the white crown lifted by the divine Uraei. 

O Gate-keeper of the one who pacifies the world, bring me offerings and support me. Let the glorious God open his arms to me, and let the Gods be silent while the Hammemit converse with me. O guide of the Gods' hearts, protect me and grant me power in heaven among the stars. Let every divinity who approaches me be counted among Ra's forerunners, the forerunners of Light and the Bright ones who adorn the sky. Let me have my will with the Bread and Beer among the Gods, enter through the Sun-disk, and come forth through the Divine Pair. Let the Gods who follow speak to me, and let Darkness and Night be terrified before me in Mehit-urit, beside the one in his Sanctuary."

Chapter CXXV: Arriving at the Hall of Righteousness

Upon arriving at the Hall of Righteousness, the deceased seeks to be absolved of all sins and to look upon the divine faces. They say: "Hail to you, mighty God, lord of Righteousness! I have come to you, my Lord, to see your glory. I know you and the names of the Forty-two Gods who appear with you in the Hall of Righteousness, devouring those who harbor mischief and drinking their blood on the Day of the searching examination in the presence of Unneferu.

'Thou of the Pair of Eyes, Lord of Righteousness' is your name. Here I am; I bring you Right and have stopped Wrong. I do not wrong others, slay my kindred, lie, commit treason, or cause mischief. I do not demand more work than is due, nor do I transgress against the God. I am not a tale-bearer, detractor, or doer of what the Gods abhor. I do not harm servants, cause famine, weep, murder, or order murder. I do not cause suffering, reduce temple offerings, or rob the dead of their funereal food. I am not an adulterer and remain undefiled in the Sanctuary of my domain's God. I do not alter grain measures, shorten the palm's length, or cut short the field's measure. I do not tamper with the balance or its tongue, snatch milk from infants, drive cattle from pastures, net birds of the Gods' manors, or catch fish from their ponds. I do not stop water at its appointed time, divide its course, extinguish lamps prematurely, or defraud the Divine Circle of their sacrificial joints. I do not drive away sacred estate cattle or stop a God when he comes forth. I am pure, like the Great Bennu in Sutenhunen, the Nose of the Lord of Air who gives life to all mortals. On the day when the Eye is full in Annu, on the last day of Mechir, in the presence of the Lord of this land, I see the Eye's fullness in Annu. Let no harm come to me in this land, in the Hall of Righteousness, because I know the names of the Gods who appear in it."

Part II: Addressing the Gods

1. "Oh thou of long strides, who appears in Annu; I am not a doer of wrong.
2. Oh thou who holds the fire, who appears in Cher-aba; I am not violent.
3. Oh thou of the Nose, who appears at Chemunnu; I am not evil-minded.
4. Oh Eater of the Shadow, who appears at Elephantine; I am not rapacious.
5. Oh thou Facing-backward God, who appears at Re-Stau; I am not a slayer of men.
6. Oh thou of Lion form, who appears in Heaven; I am not fraudulent in measures of grain.
7. Oh thou whose eyes pierce like swords, who appears in Sechem; I commit no fraud.
8. Oh thou of fiery face, whose motion is backward; I am not a robber of sacred property.
9. Oh Breaker of bones, who appears in Sutenhunen; I am not a teller of lies.
10. Oh thou who orders the flame, who appears in Memphis; I am not a robber of food.
11. Oh thou of the Two Caverns, who appears in Amenta; I am not sluggish.
12. Oh thou of the Bright Teeth, who appears in the Unseen Land; I am not a transgressor.
13. Oh Eater of Blood, who appears in the Unseen Land; I am not rapacious."Chapter CXXV: Arriving at the Hall of Righteousness

Upon arriving at the Hall of Righteousness, the deceased seeks to be absolved of all sins and to look upon the divine faces. They say: "Hail to you, mighty God, lord of Righteousness! I have come to you, my Lord, to see your glory. I know you and the names of the Forty-two Gods who appear with you in the Hall of Righteousness, devouring those who harbor mischief and drinking their blood on the Day of the searching examination in the presence of Unneferu.

'Thou of the Pair of Eyes, Lord of Righteousness' is your name. Here I am; I bring you Right and have stopped Wrong. I do not wrong others, slay my kindred, lie, commit treason, or cause mischief. I do not demand more work than is due, nor do I transgress against the God. I am not a tale-bearer, detractor, or doer of what the Gods abhor. I do not harm servants, cause famine, weep, murder, or order murder. I do not cause suffering, reduce temple offerings, or rob the dead of their funereal food. I am not an adulterer and remain undefiled in the Sanctuary of my domain's God. I do not alter grain measures, shorten the palm's length, or cut short the field's measure. I do not tamper with the balance or its tongue, snatch milk from infants, drive cattle from pastures, net birds of the Gods' manors, or catch fish from their ponds. I do not stop water at its appointed time, divide its course, extinguish lamps prematurely, or defraud the Divine Circle of their sacrificial joints. I do not drive away sacred estate cattle or stop a God when he comes forth. I am pure, like the Great Bennu in Sutenhunen, the Nose of the Lord of Air who gives life to all mortals. On the day when the Eye is full in Annu, on the last day of Mechir, in the presence of the Lord of this land, I see the Eye's fullness in Annu. Let no harm come to me in this land, in the Hall of Righteousness, because I know the names of the Gods who appear in it."

Part II: Addressing the Gods

1. "Oh thou of long strides, who appears in Annu; I am not a doer of wrong.
2. Oh thou who holds the fire, who appears in Cher-aba; I am not violent.
3. Oh thou of the Nose, who appears at Chemunnu; I am not evil-minded.
4. Oh Eater of the Shadow, who appears at Elephantine; I am not rapacious.
5. Oh thou Facing-backward God, who appears at Re-Stau; I am not a slayer of men.
6. Oh thou of Lion form, who appears in Heaven; I am not fraudulent in measures of grain.
7. Oh thou whose eyes pierce like swords, who appears in Sechem; I commit no fraud.
8. Oh thou of fiery face, whose motion is backward; I am not a robber of sacred property.
9. Oh Breaker of bones, who appears in Sutenhunen; I am not a teller of lies.
10. Oh thou who orders the flame, who appears in Memphis; I am not a robber of food.
11. Oh thou of the Two Caverns, who appears in Amenta; I am not sluggish.
12. Oh thou of the Bright Teeth, who appears in the Unseen Land; I am not a transgressor.
13. Oh Eater of Blood, who appears in the Unseen Land; I am not rapacious.
14. Oh Eater of Livers, who appears at Mabit; I deal not fraudulently.
15. Oh Lord of Righteousness, who appears in the place of Righteousness; I am not a land-grabber.
16. Oh thou who turns backwards, who appears in Bubastis; I am not an eavesdropper.
17. Oh Aati, who appears at Annu; I am not one of prating tongue.
18. Oh Tutu, who appears in Ati; I trouble myself only with my own affairs.
19. Oh Uammetu, who appears at the Block; I commit not adultery with another's wife.
20. Oh Maa-antu-f, who appears in Pa-Amsu; I am not unchaste with anyone.
21. Oh thou who art above Princes, who appears in Amu; I do not cause terrors.
22. Oh Chemiu, who appears in Kauu; I am not a transgressor.
23. Oh thou who raises thy voice, who appears in Urit; I am not hot of speech.
24. Oh divine Babe, who appears in Annu; I lend not a deaf ear to the words of Righteousness.
25. Oh high-voiced one, who appears in Unsit; I am not boisterous in behavior.
26. Oh Basit, who appears at the Shetait; I am not the cause of weeping to any.
27. Oh thou whose face is behind thee, who appears at thy cavern; I am not given to unnatural lust.
28. Oh thou, hot of foot, who appears at even; I indulge not in anger.
29. Oh Kenemtu, who appears in Kenemit; I am not given to cursing.
30. Oh thou who carries thine own offering, who appears in Syut; I am not of aggressive hand.
31. Oh thou who has different faces, who appears in Net'efit; I am not one of inconstant mind.
32. Oh Busy one, who appears at Utenit; I do not steal the skins of the sacred animals.
33. Oh thou Horned one, who appears at Sais; I am not noisy in my speech.
34. Oh Nefertmu, who appears in Memphis; I am neither a har nor a doer of mischief.
35. Oh Tem-sepu, who appears in Tattu; I am not one who curses the king.
36. Oh thou who does according to thine own will, who appears in Tebua; I put no check upon the water in its flow.
37. Oh Striker, who appears in Heaven; I am not one of loud voice.
38. Oh thou who makes mortals flourish, who appears at Sais; I curse not a God.
39. Oh thou of beautiful shoulder, who appears at ...; I am not swollen with pride.
40. Oh Neheb-kau, who appears at thy cavern; I have no unjust preferences.
41. Oh thou of raised head, who appears at thy cavern; I have no strong desire except for my own property.
42. Oh thou who lifts an arm, who appears in the Netherworld; I do not that which offends the God of my domain.

Part III: Addressing the Gods in the Tuat

Hail ye Gods, I know you and I know your names; let me not be stricken down by your blows: report not the evil which is in me to the God whom ye follow. Let not reverse of mine come to pass through you. Let not evil things be said against me in presence of the Inviolate One; because I have done the right in Tamerit. I revile not the God: let not reverse of mine come to pass through the King who resides within His own Day. Hail ye Gods who are in the Hall of Righteousness, who have nothing wrong about you; who subsist upon Righteousness in Annu, and who sate themselves with cares, in presence of the God who resides within his own Orb: deliver me from Babai who feeds upon the livers of princes on the Day of the Great Reckoning.

Behold me: I am come to you, void of wrong, without fraud, a harmless one: let me not be declared guilty; let not the issue be against me. I subsist upon Righteousness: I sate myself with uprightness of heart: I have done that which man prescribes and that which pleases the Gods. I have propitiated the God with that which he loves. I have given bread to the hungry, water to the thirsty, clothes to the naked, a boat to the shipwrecked. I have made oblations to the Gods and funeral offerings to the departed: deliver me therefore: protect me therefore: and report not against me in presence of the great God. I am one whose mouth is pure, and whose hands are pure, to whom there is said "Come, come in peace," by those who look upon him. For I have listened to the words which were spoken by the Ass and the Cat in the house of Hept-ro. And I have undergone the inspection of the God Whose face is behind him, who awards my verdict, so that I may behold what the Persea tree covers in Restau. I am one who glorifies the Gods and who knows the things which concern them. I am come and am awaiting that inquisition be made of Righteousness and that the Balance be set upon its stand within the bower of amaranth.

O thou who art exalted upon thy pedestal and who calls thy name, Lord of Air: deliver me from those messengers of thine who inflict disasters and bring about mishaps. No covering have they upon their faces. For I have done the Righteousness of a Lord of Righteousness. I have made myself pure: my front parts are washed, my back parts are pure, and my inwards steeped in the Tank of Righteousness. There is not a limb in me which is void of Righteousness. I purify me in the Southern Tank, and I rest me at the northern lake, in the Garden of Grasshoppers. The Boatmen of Ra purify them there at this hour of the night or day and the hearts of the Gods are appeased when I pass through it by night or by day. Let him come: that is what they say to me. Who, pray, art thou? that is what they say to me. What, pray, is thy name? that is what they say to me. "He who grows under the Grass and who dwells in the Olive tree" is my name. Pass on, then: that is what they say to me. I pass on to a place north of the Olive. What, prithee, didst thou see there? A thigh and a leg. And what, prithee, said they to thee? That I shall see the greetings in the lands there of the Fenchu: What, prithee, did they give to thee? A flame of fire and a pillar of crystal. And what, prithee, didst thou to them? I buried them on the bank of the Lake of Maait as Provision of the Evening. What, prithee, didst thou find there on the bank of the Lake of Maait? A sceptre of flint: 'Giver of Breath' is its name. And what didst thou to the flame of fire and to the pillar of crystal after thou hadst buried them? I cried out after them and drew them forth: and I extinguished the fire, and I broke the pillar, and I made a Tank. Thou mayest now enter through the door of the hall of Righteousness, for thou knowest us. I allow thee not to pass by me, saith the Leaf of the Door, unless thou tell my name: "The Pointer of Truth" is thy name. I allow thee not to pass by me, saith the right side post of the Door, unless thou tell my name. "The Scale-pan of one who lifts up Right" is thy name. I allow thee not to pass by me, saith the left side post of the Door, unless thou tell my name: "The Scale-pan of Wine" is thy name. I allow thee not to pass over me, saith the Threshold of the Door, unless thou tell my name: "Ox of Seb" is thy name. I open not to thee, saith the Lock of the Door, unless thou tell my name: Bone of An-maut-ef is thy name. I open not to thee, saith the Latch, unless thou tell my name: "The Eye of Sebak, Lord of Bachau," is thy name. I open not to thee, and I allow thee not to pass by me, saith the Keeper of the Door, unless thou tell my name: "The Knee of Shu, which he hath lent for the support of Osiris," is thy name. We allow thee not to pass by us, say the Lintels of the Door, unless thou tell our names: "The dragon brood of Renenut" is your name. Thou knowest us: pass therefore by us. I allow thee not to pass over me, saith the Floor of the Hall, for the reason that I am noiseless and clean, and because we know not the names of thy two feet, wherewith thou wouldst walk upon us. Tell me, then, their names. "He who goes before Amsu" is the name of my right foot: and "The Truncheon of Hathor" is the name of my left foot. Thou mayest walk over us: for thou knowest us. I do not announce thee, saith the Doorkeeper, unless thou tell my name: "He who knows the heart and explores the person" is thy name. Then I will announce thee. But who is that God who abides in his own hour? Name him. He who provides for the Two Worlds. Who, pray, is it? It is Thoth. Come hither, saith Thoth, wherefore hast thou come? I am come, and wait to be announced. And what manner of man, prithee, art thou? I have cleansed myself from all the sins and faults of those who abide in their own day; for I am no longer among them. Then I shall announce thee.Who is the one whose roof is fire, whose walls are living serpents, and whose floor is running water? It is Osiris. Proceed, for you are announced.

Your bread and beer come from the Eye, and the funeral meals offered on earth will come to you from the Eye. This is decreed for me. This chapter is recited by a person who is purified, dressed in clean clothes, wearing white sandals, anointed with oils, and presenting offerings of cattle, birds, incense, bread, beer, and vegetables. You should draw a picture on a clean brick of clay from a field where no pigs have trodden. If this chapter is written on it, the person will prosper, their children will prosper, and they will rise in the favor of the king and his court. They will receive the shesit cake, the measure of drink, the persen cake, and the meat offering on the altar table of the great God. They will not be cut off at any gate of the afterlife but will be conveyed along with the Kings of North and South and appear as a follower of Osiris, unwaveringly and for all time.

Chapter CXXV Notes:
This chapter is significant for understanding the religion and ethics of ancient Egypt. The oldest copies date back to the eighteenth dynasty, but the chapter is likely much older. It consists of three parts, with the second part growing out of the first, suggested by the mention of the "Forty-two" Gods and the "negative confession" of certain sins. The second part lists Gods and associated sins, totaling forty-two, which is more than in Part I. The two lists agree to some extent but also differ, indicating they were created by different authors or at different times.

Part III involves an extraordinary examination of the deceased, which is not indicated in Part I. The picture of the Psychostasia (weighing of the heart) is considered part of the chapter, though the texts on it vary and have no canonical authority. The order of trials and the moment the deceased is freed from anxiety about their fate are not consistent across these documents, suggesting they are independent compositions brought together by their subject matter.

Artists of the Ramseside period added another scene where the deceased is judged by a smaller group of Gods, not the forty-two assessors of Osiris. The essential idea is a trial before Osiris, where the person's conduct or conscience is weighed. This trial is mentioned in various chapters of the Book of the Dead and other texts, showing that scribes and artists had creative freedom regarding the details.

The number of forty-two assessors might be linked to the Nomes of Egypt, but this is only certain for later periods and not earlier times. The localities where the Gods appear do not correspond to the Nomes, and some are not earthly locations. For example, Annu is the Eastern Solar Mountain where the Sun rises, not Heliopolis of Egypt. Similarly, Chemunnu is not Hermopolis of Egypt but the place of the Eight Gods who help the Sun rise.

The assessors are not evil spirits but Gods who accompany Osiris and represent his Names, Limbs, and Body. They are strict and just, embodying the concept of Maat (Righteousness), which is about moral conduct and conformity to the Rule of Right. The "Hall of Righteousness" is a dual space where the Sun-God is present, divided into two parts on either side of the God. The two Goddesses Maat, representing Isis and Nephthys, are on the left and right sides of Osiris.

In summary, the chapter emphasizes the importance of moral conduct and righteousness, with the deceased undergoing trials and examinations to prove their worthiness before Osiris and the Gods.The current chapter presents numerous challenges, and while some issues have been addressed, many remain unresolved. Champollion's early work on this chapter sparked significant interest, but the complexities he didn't tackle have only grown with our advancing understanding of the language and its scientific analysis. The text is highly uncertain in key areas, with variations in the list of forty-two sins across different manuscripts, and inconsistencies in the Gods assigned to judge these sins. Notably, the Sutimes papyrus omits certain sins that an Egyptian would typically be expected to account for. Additionally, the same word can appear with different meanings in the same passage across various papyri. Some important words were initially guessed by early translators and have been retained without sufficient justification by their successors. This translation is offered with full awareness of its imperfections and the ongoing challenges in achieving a satisfactory version.

Dr. Pleyte made a significant contribution to our understanding of the first part of the chapter in 1866 with his "Etudes Egyptologiques." Since then, other versions have been produced by Deveria, Lefebure, and Pierret. The Demotic text of the chapter, first published by Brugsch and more recently with a complete translation by M. Revillout, is fascinating but, being from the Roman imperial period, cannot always be relied upon as an authoritative interpretation of the ancient text.

The term "Day of searching examination or reckoning" is derived from the concept of a circle, symbolizing a thorough investigation from all sides. Osiris, often referred to as "He of the Pair of Eyes," is associated with the Goddesses Isis and Nephthys, who are depicted in various forms in the chapter's vignettes. These Goddesses symbolize the Two Eyes, a frequent motif on funerary monuments, representing the light at dawn and sunset, as well as the light cast by the sun throughout its journey.

The symbol of the Two Eyes is deeply significant, appearing on ancient coffins, mummy cases, and funerary tablets, often accompanied by other symbols like the sign of water or the vase, which are also emblems of Osiris. The Two Eyes are sometimes represented within the Winged Disk, and Osiris is frequently depicted with these symbols, highlighting his connection to eternity and renewal.

Isis and Nephthys, as mythological figures, represent not just specific times of day but the entire course of the sun, both in the heavens and the underworld. The expression "he lightens up the earth with his two eyes" is common in texts and emphasizes their role in illuminating the world. When the Two Eyes are distinguished from each other, the symbolism changes, with the Right Eye often associated with Ra and the Left Eye with Osiris.

The chapter also discusses the concept of the Full Moon and its association with Osiris, as well as the idea of the Full Sun, which is linked to the Winter Solstice. This complex symbolism underscores the intricate beliefs and rituals surrounding Osiris and the afterlife in ancient Egyptian religion.In ancient Egyptian texts, the Winter Solstice is often referenced, and in 1470 B.C., the Egyptian year started on July 20, with January 15 marking the 30th Mechir in the Julian calendar. The Eye, symbolizing the Sun, was considered full at the Winter Solstice and likely at the Summer Solstice and both Equinoxes. This is why images of the Four Rudders of Heaven (North, South, East, and West) often feature an Eye on each rudder (see Chapter 148 vignettes). The Two Eyes, representing the Sun and Moon, are attributed to Ra and Osiris, and other Gods associated with them. For instance, the phrase "Thy Right Eye is the Sun and thy left is the Moon" is addressed to Ptah in the Papyrus Berlin, and a similar phrase on the Neapolitan Stele refers to Osiris as the Ram-headed deity Her-s'efit. The Pyramid Texts mention Horus having a Light and a Dark eye, with the "Eye of Horus" usually symbolizing the Sun. This eye is linked to prosperity and abundance, symbolized by offerings like cakes, ale, wine, corn, oil, and honey.

Many Goddesses bear the title "Eye of Ra," all identified with Isis or Nephthys, symbolizing the Sun's light. Shu and Tefnut, brother and sister, play similar roles. In Chapter 17 vignettes, two deities with Eyes over their heads are depicted, possibly representing Thoth, the Sun, and the Moon. The Sun and Moon as Eyes of Osiris, Ra, or Ptah are manifestations of these deities, not to be confused with the deities themselves.

The text also discusses various sins and their divine judges. For example, the crime of killing kindred is highlighted, with historical references to similar acts in various cultures. The term "kindred" doesn't necessarily imply multiple people. The text also addresses the concept of truth, with different translations suggesting either "instead of truth" or "not telling lies in the cemetery." The Pyramid Texts use the phrase "Right instead of Wrong."

Other passages discuss the imposition of labor, the measurement of fields, and the balance beam used in weighing. The balance beam, often depicted in Egyptian art, is described with a tongue and plummet to ensure accuracy. Fraudulent practices involving the balance are mentioned, with the plummet symbolizing a cup full of liquid.

The text also refers to the "manors of the Gods," property acquired by royal grant, and the deceased acquiring land in the afterlife. The term "ponds" is correctly read as a specific type of land feature, with some papyri mistakenly using other determinatives. The God Thoth, identified by his beak, is mentioned, along with the "Eater of the Shadow," possibly referring to gnomons without shadows at noon during the Summer Solstice. The lion-headed God, sluggishness, and other divine attributes are also discussed.In ancient Egyptian texts, the Winter Solstice is often referenced. In 1470 B.C., the Egyptian year started on July 20, with January 15 marking the 30th Mechir in the Julian calendar. The Eye, symbolizing the Sun, was considered full at the Winter Solstice and likely at the Summer Solstice and both Equinoxes.
